But maybe we can avoid having the leftover set as the points not covered by the non-sunny lines? We can choose the non-sunny lines to cover a different set, not necessarily the "boundary" points. The idea is that we need to cover all points with n lines. The total number of points is n(n+1)/2. With n lines, each line can cover many points. The covering problem is like: we need to select n lines (some with restricted slopes) that cover all points. The question is: for which k does such a covering exist? We need to find all k for which there exists a set of n lines covering P, with exactly k lines having slopes not in {0,∞,-1}.

Let's try to think more generally. The points P form a triangular grid. We can consider the lines that are "natural" for covering such points: the vertical lines x=i, horizontal lines y=j, and the diagonals x+y=c. These lines are all non-sunny. And we have exactly n of them: for each i=1..n, x=i covers n+1-i points; for each j=1..n, y=j covers n+1-j points; for each c=2..n+1, x+y=c covers c-1 points (since positive integers, a+b=c, a,b≥1, so number of points is c-1). So these families each consist of n lines that cover all points. In fact, any of these families alone covers P. So we have three trivial coverings with all lines non-sunny (k=0). So k=0 always works.

Because the points P are in a triangular grid of size n; the collinearities with slope not 0,∞,-1 are limited. Actually, there is a known fact: For points with positive integer coordinates in a right triangle, the maximum number of collinear points is n (for lines of slope -1), n (for vertical lines), n (for horizontal lines), and 2 for other slopes. Indeed, the points are (a,b) with a+b≤n+1. For a line with slope m, if m is not -1, the intersection with the lattice points can be at most 2 because the equation a + b = constant gives collinear points with slope -1; vertical lines are a constant; horizontal lines are b constant. For other slopes, the line cannot contain more than 2 points because the points are not in arithmetic progression in both coordinates? Let's verify more generally. Suppose we have three distinct points (a1,b1),(a2,b2),(a3,b3) in P that are collinear, with slope m ≠ 0,∞,-1. Then the differences satisfy (b2-b1)/(a2-a1) = (b3-b1)/(a3-a1) = m. Since the points are integer, the differences are integers. So m is rational. But can we have three collinear points with the same rational slope? For the triangle region, if the slope is not -1, it is unlikely. Consider the line equation: b = m a + c. If it passes through three points with integer a, then the b values are integer. For a1<a2<a3, the differences in b are m times differences in a. For m rational, say m = p/q in lowest terms, then b differences must be multiples of p/q times integer differences. For the b differences to be integers, the a differences must be multiples of q. So we could have three points with a coordinates in arithmetic progression with common difference q, and b values also in arithmetic progression with common difference p. So there could be many collinear points if the points are in arithmetic progression. For example, points (1,1),(4,4) slope 1; can we have (2,2) and (3,3)? But (3,3) is not in the triangle if n=4? For n=4, triangle includes a+b≤5, so (3,3) gives 6>5, not in P. So the region restricts. In general, for a given n, the maximum number of collinear points with slope m not 0,∞,-1 is at most 2 because the points lie on a line that cannot contain more than 2 points from the triangular grid? Let's test for n=5 (points with a+b≤6). Could there be a line with slope 1 that contains three points? For example, (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) all have a+b=2,3,6? Actually (1,1): sum=2; (2,2): sum=4; (3,3): sum=6. For n=5, allowed sums ≤6, so (3,3) is allowed (since 3+3=6). So (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are all in P. And they are collinear on y=x. So that's 3 points. So for n=5, a line with slope 1 can contain 3 points. So the maximum is not always 2; it depends on n. In fact, for slope 1, the points (a,a) are in P as long as 2a ≤ n+1, so a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of such points is about n/2. So a line of slope 1 can contain many points. Similarly, slope 2: points (a,2a) are in P if 3a ≤ n+1, so a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So it can contain floor((n+1)/3) points. So lines with slopes that are not -1 can contain many points, but they are "sunny" only if slope not 0,∞,-1. So we need to consider that lines can be "dense" with points. In the covering problem, we can use lines that contain many lattice points, which can be sunny (if slope not 0,∞,-1). So the construction for k=3 for n=4 used lines that each covered 2 points; but for n=5, we might be able to use a line of slope 1 covering many points. So the earlier failure for k=2 might be due to n=4 being small; for larger n, k=2 might be possible.

We need to determine all possible k for given n≥3. So we need to find necessary conditions and sufficient constructions.

Let's think about the problem more abstractly. We have a set of points P (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. We need to cover them with n lines. Exactly k of these lines are sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1). The other n-k lines are non-sunny: they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The total number of lines is n.

We can think of each line as a set of points. The covering condition means that the union of these n lines equals P.

We need to find all k for which such a covering exists.

Observations:
- The three families of non-sunny lines (vertical, horizontal, slope -1) each individually cover P with n lines. So we can always have k=0.

- For k=1, we can take n-1 vertical lines (non-sunny) and 1 sunny line that goes through the single point not covered by the vertical lines (the point (n,1)). That works for any n≥3. So k=1 is always possible.

- For k=n (all lines sunny), we need to cover all points with n sunny lines. Is that always possible? We need to find a set of n lines, each with slope not 0,∞,-1, that together cover P. For n=3, we gave an example with 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. For n=4, we need to cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines. Can we do it? Possibly by using lines that are "diagonals" of the triangle. For example, consider the lines: y = x, y = -x+5? But y=-x+5 is slope -1, not sunny. So not allowed. Perhaps use lines with slopes 1, 2, 3, ...? But we need exactly n lines, and each line can cover many points. Let's attempt to construct for n=4. We need 4 sunny lines covering all points. Could we use the three lines from the k=3 construction (which covered 6 points) and then one more line that covers the remaining 4 points. The remaining points are on x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). A single sunny line cannot cover all four because they are vertical. But could we design the three lines to cover more points, so that the remaining points are fewer? For example, maybe we can use a line of slope 1 that covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) but (4,4) not in P (since 4+4=8>5). So slope 1 line covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (3 points). That's good. Then we need to cover the other 7 points. Could we use two more sunny lines? But we need total 4 lines. So after using one slope 1 line, we have 3 lines left. Possibly we can use a line of slope -2 that covers some points, etc. Let's try systematic.

We can treat the covering problem as: we want to select n lines (some with restricted slopes) that cover all points. The points are lattice points in a triangle. The lines can be chosen arbitrarily; we are not forced to use only lines that pass through lattice points, but it's convenient to use lines that contain many lattice points.

We can consider the concept of "sunny lines" as lines that are not parallel to the axes or to x+y=0. So they have slopes m such that m is defined and m ≠ 0, ∞, -1. So they are "generic" lines.

We need to determine which k can be achieved. Possibly the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1? Or maybe all k from 0 to n are possible? For n=3, we saw k=2 impossible, so not all. For n=4, we need to test k=2, maybe k=2 is impossible? Let's try to construct for n=4, k=2. We need 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Total lines = 4. Can we find such a covering? Let's attempt to design.

We need to cover all 10 points. The two non-sunny lines can be, say, vertical x=1 and x=2? That covers (1,1)-(1,4) and (2,1)-(2,3). That's 4+3=7 points. Remaining: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 3 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. As before, any line through two of these points is non-sunny (since (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (3,1)-(3,2) vertical; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1). So each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. So we need 3 sunny lines to cover 3 points. But we have only 2. So that fails.

What if we use 1 vertical and 1 horizontal? For instance, x=1 covers 4 points; y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). But (1,1) is common. So union: (1,1)-(1,4) and (2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That's 4+3=7 points (since (1,1) counted once). Remaining: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover them. Check pairs: (2,2)-(2,3) vertical x=2 (non-sunny); (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal y=2 (non-sunny); (2,3)-(3,2) slope = (2-3)/(3-2)=-1 (non-sunny). So no pair is sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point, need 3 lines, but only 2. So fails.

What if we use 1 slope -1 and 1 vertical? For example, x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)) and x=1 (covers (1,1)-(1,4)). Union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Remaining: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. Again, any line through two of these is non-sunny: (2,1)-(2,2) vertical; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1. So cannot cover with 2 sunny lines.

What if we use 1 slope -1 and 1 horizontal? x+y=5 and y=1: union: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) from x+y=5; (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) from y=1. So points: (1,1),(1,4),(2,1),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Remaining: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). 3 points. Again, no sunny pair.

What if we use 2 vertical lines? x=1 and x=2: as before, 7 points, remaining 3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). No sunny pair.

What if we use 2 horizontal lines? y=1 and y=2: union: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) and (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). That's 7 points. Remaining: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). 3 points. No sunny pair? Check (1,3)-(2,3) horizontal y=3 non-sunny; (1,3)-(1,4) vertical; (1,4)-(2,3) slope = (3-4)/(2-1)=-1 non-sunny. So no.

What if we use 1 vertical and 1 slope -1 but choose a different vertical line? For n=4, we have vertical lines x=1,2,3,4. If we use x=2 (covers (2,1)-(2,3)) and x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)), union: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) from x=2; (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) from x+y=5. So points: (1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 6 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(3,1). That's 4 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover these 4 points. Could we cover these 4 points with 2 sunny lines? Let's examine the set S = {(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(3,1)}. As before, any line through two of these points: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical; (1,1)-(1,3) vertical; (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (1,2)-(3,1) slope = (1-2)/(3-1)=-1/2 (sunny); (1,3)-(3,1) slope = (1-3)/(3-1)=-2/2=-1 non-sunny; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical; (1,2)-(3,1) slope -1/2 sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1 non-sunny. So there are sunny lines: (1,2)-(3,1) and (1,3)-(3,1) is non-sunny; (1,1)-(3,1) is horizontal; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical. So the only sunny pair is (1,2) and (3,1). That line covers those two points. Then we still have (1,1) and (1,3) uncovered. Can we cover them with one more sunny line? They are vertical, so any line through them is vertical, not sunny. Could a sunny line cover (1,1) and (1,3)? That would be vertical, not allowed. Could a sunny line cover (1,1) and (3,1)? That would be horizontal, not allowed. Could a sunny line cover (1,1) and (1,2)? That's vertical. So no. So with 2 sunny lines, we cannot cover 4 points that are not collinear with sunny slope. Actually we have 4 points; we need to cover them with 2 lines. If we use L1: through (1,2) and (3,1) (suny), it covers 2 points. Then we have 2 points left: (1,1) and (1,3). They are not collinear with any sunny line? Could we have L2: some line through (1,1) that also covers (1,3)? That's vertical, not sunny. Or through (1,1) and (3,1)? Horizontal. Or through (1,1) and some other point outside S? But we need to cover (1,1) and (1,3) as part of the union of lines; they could be covered by a line that also covers other points already covered by previous lines. For example, we could have L2: line through (1,1) and (2,3) which is not in S? But (2,3) is already covered by x=2 or x+y=5? Actually (2,3) is covered by x=2 (since x=2, b=3) and also by x+y=5? 2+3=5, so yes. So L2 could pass through (1,1) and (2,3). Slope = (3-1)/(2-1)=2, sunny. That line would cover (1,1) and (2,3). But (2,3) is already covered, but that's fine; we need to cover (1,1) and (1,3). So L2 covers (1,1) but not (1,3). So we still need to cover (1,3). Could we have L2 covering (1,1) and (1,3)? No. So we would need a third sunny line for (1,3). But we have only 2 sunny lines. So not possible.

What if we use a different non-sunny line pair? Maybe use 1 slope -1 and 1 horizontal, but choose a different horizontal line? For n=4, horizontal lines: y=1,2,3,4. y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3) (since 3+2=5? Actually a+b≤5: for y=3, a can be 1,2 because 1+3=4≤5, 2+3=5≤5, a=3 gives 6>5). So y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3). y=4 covers (1,4) only. So if we use y=3 and x+y=5? That gives union: from x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); from y=3: (1,3),(2,3). So points: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 5 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 5 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover 5 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from this set? Possibly if they are collinear with sunny slope. Let's examine the set R = {(1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1)}. Are there sunny collinear triples? Check (1,1)-(2,2): slope 1, covers (1,1),(2,2). Could also pass through (3,3) not present. So 2 points. (1,2)-(2,1): slope -1, non-sunny. (1,2)-(3,1): slope = (1-2)/(3-1)=-1/2 sunny, covers (1,2),(3,1). (2,1)-(3,1): horizontal. (1,1)-(2,1): horizontal. (1,1)-(3,1): horizontal. (2,2)-(3,1): slope = (1-2)/(3-2)=-1 non-sunny. So possible sunny pairs: (1,1)-(2,2) and (1,2)-(3,1). So we could have L1: through (1,1) and (2,2) (slope 1), covering those two; L2: through (1,2) and (3,1) (slope -1/2), covering those two; but then points (2,1) and (2,2) maybe? Actually (2,2) already covered by L1; (2,1) remains. (2,1) not covered by L1 or L2. Could we cover (2,1) by a line that also covers some other points? But we have only two sunny lines; we need to cover all 5 points. So we need a third sunny line, not allowed. Could we adjust the non-sunny lines to cover more points? For instance, use y=2 and x+y=5? That gave 7 points covered? Let's try other combos.

Maybe we can use a non-sunny line that is not "boundary" but covers many points, and then use two sunny lines that cover the rest, but we have to use exactly 2 sunny lines. The total number of points is 10. With 2 sunny lines, each can cover at most some number of points, but if the non-sunny lines cover many, the leftover might be covered by the 2 sunny lines. However, the leftover points are often not collinear in a sunny way. We need to see if there exists a selection of n lines with k=2 that covers all points.

Perhaps for n=4, k=2 is impossible. Let's try to prove a general necessary condition.

Consider the points in P. We have n lines total. For each line, define its "type". Non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Sunny lines are other slopes. The covering condition imposes constraints on the number of lines of each type.

Idea: Use a combinatorial double counting or a parity argument. Consider the "sum of coordinates" s = a+b. Points have s from 2 to n+1. The lines that are not sunny have a special property: horizontal lines are all points with a given b; vertical lines are all points with a given a; slope -1 lines are all points with a+b constant. For sunny lines, they are not of these types.

Perhaps we can consider the set of points that are covered by a line of type horizontal or vertical or slope -1. These lines can be thought of as "grid lines". The points on these lines form a "grid". The points that are not on any such line must be covered by sunny lines. So the sunny lines must cover all points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines.

Let N be the set of non-sunny lines. They are a collection of horizontal, vertical, and slope -1 lines. Each such line is a set of points. The union of these lines is some subset of P. Let U be that union. Then the remaining points R = P \ U must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines are n - k lines. So we need to cover R with n - k sunny lines.

Now, what are the possible structures of R? The non-sunny lines can be chosen arbitrarily. The points P are in a triangle. The grid lines (vertical/horizontal) partition the triangle. The slope -1 lines are also "diagonals". So we have a combinatorial design problem.

Perhaps we can find that the minimal number of sunny lines needed to cover the points not covered by a certain set of non-sunny lines is at least something. Then we can find which k allow that.

Alternatively, maybe the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1? But for n=3, n-1=2 is impossible, so that fits. For n=4, we need to test if k=3 works (we already found an example), k=2 might be impossible, k=4 might be possible? Let's test k=4 for n=4. Can we cover all 10 points with 4 sunny lines? Let's attempt to construct.

We need 4 lines, each with slope not 0,∞,-1. They must cover all points. Since we have 4 lines, each line can contain multiple points. Could we use the three lines from the k=3 example (x=1 non-sunny) but now we need all lines sunny. So we cannot use x=1. So we need a different covering. Let's attempt to use lines that are "sunny" and cover many points. For n=4, the points are:

(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4)
(2,1),(2,2),(2,3)
(3,1),(3,2)
(4,1)

We need to cover them with 4 sunny lines. A natural idea: Use lines that are "diagonals" of the triangle, but not slope -1. For example, line y = x (slope 1) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) not in P. So it covers (1,1),(2,2). That's only 2 points. Line y = 2x? covers (1,2),(2,4) but (2,4) not in P. So only (1,2). Not many. Line y = -2x+? maybe covers (1,3),(2,1) slope -2, covers 2 points. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points? For n=4, the triangle is small. Could there be a sunny line that contains 3 points? For slope 1, points (a,a) are in P for a=1,2,3? (3,3) not present because 3+3=6>5. So only 2. For slope 2, points (a,2a) for a=1,2: (1,2),(2,4) but (2,4) not in P. So only 1. For slope 1/2? Points (2a,a) would be (2,1),(4,2) but (4,2) not in P (4+2=6>5). So only 1. So it seems that for n=4, any sunny line can contain at most 2 points. So with 4 sunny lines, we can cover at most 8 points (if each covers 2 distinct points). But we have 10 points. So it's impossible for n=4, k=4. So k=4 fails.

Now, what about k=2 for n=4? We attempted many and failed. Could it be that k=2 is impossible for n=4? Let's try to systematically see if we can cover all points with 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. We need to select 2 non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) and 2 sunny lines. Their union must be P. Let's denote the two non-sunny lines as L1 and L2. They cover some points. The remaining points must be covered by the two sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most 2 points (as argued), the remaining points must be at most 4. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least 6 points. For n=4, total points=10, so the non-sunny lines must cover at least 6 points. Can two non-sunny lines cover 6 or more points? Let's compute the maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover. For vertical line x=i, it covers n+1-i points. For n=4, x=1:4 points; x=2:3 points; x=3:2 points; x=4:1 point. Horizontal line y=j: y=1:4 points; y=2:3 points; y=3:2 points; y=4:1 point. Slope -1 line x+y=c: c=2:1 point (1,1); c=3:2 points (1,2),(2,1); c=4:3 points (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); c=5:4 points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So the maximum points on a non-sunny line is 4. Two such lines can cover at most 4+4=8 points, but they might overlap. So the union can be at most 8 points. But we need them to cover at least 6 points. So it's possible. For example, two vertical lines x=1 and x=2 cover 4+3=7 points. That's 7 points. The remaining 3 points (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) must be covered by 2 sunny lines. As we saw, those 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any line through two of them is non-sunny, and each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. So that fails. What about two horizontal lines y=1 and y=2 cover 4+3=7 points, remaining (1,3),(1,4),(2,3) (3 points) also cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. Two slope -1 lines: x+y=5 and x+y=4 cover 4+3=7 points, but their union: x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Union: (1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1) = 7 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). 3 points, cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. Two vertical lines x=1 and x=3: x=1 gives 4; x=3 gives 2; total 6, overlapping? x=3 points: (3,1),(3,2); x=1 points: (1,1)-(1,4); no overlap. So union 6 points. Remaining: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(4,1) = 4 points. Need to cover these 4 points with 2 sunny lines. Is that possible? Let's examine set R = {(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(4,1)}. Can we find two sunny lines covering all these? Check pairs: (2,1)-(2,2) vertical x=2 non-sunny; (2,1)-(2,3) vertical; (2,1)-(4,1) horizontal y=1 non-sunny; (2,2)-(2,3) vertical; (2,2)-(4,1) slope = (1-2)/(4-2)=-1/2 sunny; (2,3)-(4,1) slope = (1-3)/(4-2)=-2/2=-1 non-sunny. So only sunny pair is (2,2) and (4,1). That line covers those two. Then we still have (2,1) and (2,3) left. Could we cover them with one more sunny line? They are vertical, so not sunny. Could we have a line through (2,1) and (2,3)? That's vertical, not sunny. Or through (2,1) and (4,1)? Horizontal, not sunny. Or through (2,3) and (4,1)? Slope -1, not sunny. So no. Could we have a different selection of the two sunny lines that covers more points? Possibly we could have one sunny line covering three points? But we argued each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. Could it cover 3? For n=4, check line through (2,1) and (2,2) is vertical, not sunny. Through (2,1) and (4,1) horizontal. Through (2,2) and (4,1) slope -0.5, covers 2 points. Could it also pass through (2,1)? No. So no triple. So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 4 points if each covers 2 distinct points and they are disjoint. But we have 4 points in R; we could try to have L1 cover (2,1) and (2,2)? No. L1 cover (2,1) and (4,1)? No. L1 cover (2,2) and (4,1) (2 points); L2 cover (2,1) and (2,3)? Not sunny. L2 cover (2,1) and (4,1)? Horizontal. So no. So fails.

What about two non-sunny lines that are one vertical and one slope -1? x=1 and x+y=5: union 7 points, remaining 3 points cannot be covered. x=1 and x+y=4: x=1 gives 4, x+y=4 gives 3, union: (1,1)-(1,4) and (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) => (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. Remaining: (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 points. Need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. Check set: {(2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)}. Are there sunny pairs? (2,1)-(2,3) vertical; (2,1)-(3,2) slope = (2-1)/(3-2)=1 (sunny); (2,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (2,3)-(3,2) slope = (2-3)/(3-2)=-1 non-sunny; (2,3)-(4,1) slope = (1-3)/(4-2)=-2/2=-1 non-sunny; (3,2)-(4,1) slope = (1-2)/(4-3)=-1 non-sunny. So only sunny pair is (2,1)-(3,2). That line covers those two. Then we have (2,3) and (4,1) left. Can we cover them with one sunny line? They are (2,3)-(4,1): slope -1, not sunny. So no.

What about x=2 and x+y=5? x=2 gives 3, x+y=5 gives 4, union: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) from x=2; (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) from x+y=5 => (1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) (since (2,3) common). That's 5 points? Actually count: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(1,4),(3,2),(4,1) => 6 points. So union 6 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(3,1). That's 4 points. Need to cover with 2 sunny lines. Set R = {(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(3,1)}. As before, only sunny pair is (1,2)-(3,1) slope -1/2. That covers 2 points, leaves (1,1) and (1,3) which are vertical, cannot be covered by sunny line. So fails.

What about x=2 and x+y=4? x=2 gives 3, x+y=4 gives 3, union: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) and (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) => (1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1). That's 5 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,4),(3,2),(4,1). That's 5 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover 5 points, impossible since each covers at most 2. So fails.

What about two slope -1 lines: x+y=4 and x+y=5 gave union 7 points, remaining 3. x+y=3 and x+y=5: x+y=3 gives 2 points (1,2),(2,1); x+y=5 gives 4 points; union 6 points (since no overlap? (1,2),(2,1) not in x+y=5). So union 6 points. Remaining: (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)? Wait total points: 10. Union: from x+y=3: (1,2),(2,1); from x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Given the difficulty, maybe we need to find a general characterization. Let's step back and think about the problem more systematically.

The points P = {(a,b) in Z^2: a,b >= 1, a+b <= n+1}. We want to cover them with n lines. Each line is either sunny (slope not 0, ∞, -1) or non-sunny (slope 0, ∞, or -1). Let's denote the number of lines of each type. We have n lines total. The problem asks for which k (number of sunny lines) does such a covering exist.

Observations:
- The three families of non-sunny lines (vertical, horizontal, slope -1) each individually cover P with n lines. So k=0 always works.
- For any n, we can take n-1 vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 and one sunny line through (n,1). That covers all points. So k=1 always works.
- For k=n, we need all lines sunny. Is that always possible? For n=3, yes. For n=4, we found impossible because each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, and we need 10 points with 4 lines, but the maximum distinct points covered by 4 lines with at most 2 points each is 8, and we have 10 points, so impossible. For n=5, each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so with 5 lines we could cover at most 15 points, which is exactly the number of points. So we need a partition of the 15 points into 5 collinear triples with sunny slopes. We attempted to find such a partition and argued that it's impossible because the remaining 6 points after using three triples (slope1, slope-1/2, slope-2) could not be covered by two triples, but we need to check if there exists any partition into 5 triples. Perhaps we can use a different set of triples. For n=5, we have points with a+b ≤6. Let's list all possible collinear triples with sunny slopes. For slope 1, points (a,a) for a=1,2,3: that's triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). For slope -1/2, points (a, -a/2 + c) we need integer coordinates. For example, line through (1,3),(3,2),(5,1) slope -1/2: that's triple (1,3),(3,2),(5,1). For slope -2, line through (1,5),(2,3),(3,1): triple (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). For slope 2, points (1,2),(2,4) only 2 points; (3,6) not in P. So no triple. For slope 1/2, points (2,1),(4,2) only 2; (6,3) not. So no triple. For slope -1/3, points (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So no triple. For slope 3, points (1,3) only; (2,6) no. So no triple. For slope -3, points (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So only three triples are possible. Therefore we cannot partition the 15 points into 5 triples. So k=n=5 impossible.

For n=6, points with a+b ≤7, total points = 1+2+3+4+5+6 = 21. Each sunny line can contain at most? For slope 1, points (a,a) for a=1..3? 2a ≤7 => a≤3.5, so a=1,2,3: triple. For slope 1/2, (2,1),(4,2),(6,3) sum=9? 6+3=9>7, so only (2,1),(4,2) maybe. For slope -1/2, (1,5),(3,4),(5,3),(7,2) but (7,2) sum=9>7, so (1,5),(3,4),(5,3) triple? Check: (1,5): sum=6, (3,4): sum=7, (5,3): sum=8>7, so (5,3) not in P. So only (1,5),(3,4) maybe. For slope -2, (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) triple? (1,5) sum=6, (2,3) sum=5, (3,1) sum=4, all ≤7, yes triple. For slope -3, (1,4),(2,1) only. So maximum points on a sunny line is 3 (slope 1, slope -2, etc.). So with 6 sunny lines, maximum distinct points covered is 18, but we have 21 points. So k=n=6 impossible. In fact, for general n, what is the maximum number of points on a sunny line? A line with slope m (rational, not 0,∞,-1) that passes through many points of P. The points P are in a triangle. For a given rational slope m, the points on the line are those (a,b) with b = m a + c, and a,b integers, a,b>=1, a+b≤n+1. The number of such points is at most something like floor((n+1)/(1+m))? Actually, if m>0, then b = m a + c, and a+b = a + m a + c = (1+m)a + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ (n+1-c)/(1+m). Also a>=1. So the number of integer a is about (n+1)/(1+m). For m=1, we get a ≤ (n+1-c)/2, so max 3 points if c is chosen appropriately (c=1,2,3? So k=n=5 fails.

Thus we need to find all k for which there exists a covering with exactly k sunny lines. The problem likely expects a characterization like: k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1 and n? Actually n=4, n-1=3 is present, n-2=2 is missing, n=4 missing. So pattern: for n=4, k=0,1,3 (3 is n-1). For n=3, k=0,1,3 (3 is n). So it's not simply all except n-1.

Maybe we need to consider the general case. Let's think about the covering problem in terms of "lines" that are either "sunny" or "non-sunny". The points P have a triangular structure. We can try to use a construction that uses a certain number of non-sunny lines to cover a "grid" and then use sunny lines to cover the remaining points. That's 11 points. So the missing points are: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1),(4,? ) actually (4,?) (4,1) and (4,2) are covered; (5,1) covered. So missing: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1). That's 4 points. Yes, that's correct. So leftover = {(1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1)}. Now, can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? Points: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1). Check slopes: (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal y=1 non-sunny; (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (1,1)-(1,3) vertical; (1,3)-(2,1) slope -2 sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope -1 non-sunny; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal. So only sunny pair is (1,3)-(2,1) slope -2. That covers two points, leaves (1,1) and (3,1). Can we cover them with a sunny line? (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal y=1 non-sunny. So no. So fails.

Thus for n=5, we haven't found a configuration for k=2. Maybe it's impossible.

Given the analysis, it seems that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. Let's attempt to prove it generally.

We have n lines. Let s be the number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - s. The total points is N = n(n+1)/2. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They can cover at most some points. We need to cover all points. For k=2, we need s = n-2. The maximum number of distinct points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines is achieved by taking n-2 lines of one type that are pairwise disjoint. For vertical lines, the sizes are n, n-1, ..., 1. So the sum of the largest n-2 numbers is sum_{i=1}^{n-2} (n+1-i) = sum_{j=3}^{n} j = n(n+1)/2 - 3. So maximum union = N - 3. So we can cover at most N - 3 points with n-2 non-sunny lines. Then leftover points are at least 3. But we need to cover those leftover points with 2 sunny lines. However, the leftover points are exactly the points not on the selected lines. We can choose the non-sunny lines in many ways; we are not forced to use the maximal union. For k=2, we need to select n-2 non-sunny lines such that the union size is at most N (obviously). We need the union to be N minus the number of points that will be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines will cover the complement. So we need to cover some set of points (the complement) with 2 sunny lines. That complement must be a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. So we need to find a set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement is coverable by 2 sunny lines. Since we have freedom to choose the non-sunny lines, we can try to make the complement small (like 4 points) and also coverable.

But note: The total number of lines is n. If we use n-2 non-sunny lines, we have 2 sunny lines left. So we need to cover all points in P. The non-sunny lines cover some points. The sunny lines must cover the remaining points. So we need to find a selection of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the remaining points (the ones not on these lines) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That is the problem.

We attempted for n=5 to find such a selection. We found one that gave complement of 4 points, but those 4 points were not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could there be a selection that gives complement of 3 points? Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points, which is impossible because each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them (since any line through two of them is non-sunny). So complement cannot be 3. Could it be 4? Then we need to check if any set of 4 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That is possible if the 4 points can be partitioned into two sunny collinear sets. For example, a set like {(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4)} can be covered by 1 sunny line (y=x). That's 1 line, but we have 2 lines; we could also cover it with 2 lines (e.g., y=x and y=x-2). So any set that is a union of two sunny lines works. So we need to find if there exists a set of 4 points that is a union of two sunny lines, and also such that the complement (the other 11 points for n=5) can be covered by 3 non-sunny lines. That is the existence.

We tried several sets of 4 points that are unions of two sunny lines, e.g., points on y=x and y=x-2. For n=5, the set of points on y=x with sum≤6: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum=6 is in P, so triple. On y=x-2: points (3,1),(4,2) but (5,3) sum=8>6, so only two points. So the union would be {(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(3,1),(4,2)}? That's 5 points, not 4. To get exactly 4 points, we need a set of 4 points that is a union of two sunny lines, each with at most 2 points? Actually if each line has 2 points, union size 4. For n=5, can we find two sunny lines each containing exactly 2 points from P such that the lines are distinct and the union is a set of 4 points? For instance, line y=x+1: points (1,2),(2,3) are in P (sums 3,5). Line y=x-1: points (2,1),(3,2) are in P (sums 3,5). Their union is {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2)} which are 4 points. Are these points coverable by 2 sunny lines? Yes, the lines themselves are sunny. So that set works. Can we cover the complement (the other 11 points) with 3 non-sunny lines? The complement would be all points except these 4. Points for n=5: list them. The set we want to leave uncovered is S = {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2)}. The other points: (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5); (2,4); (3,1),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). That's 1+2+1+2+1+1+1 =? Given the time, it's more efficient to look for a general result. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n≥3 be an integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both: for all positive integers a,b with a+b≤n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." The answer might be: k can be 0,1,3, and maybe also n? But for n=3, k=3 is n; for n=4, k=3 is n-1; for n=5, k=3 is n-2; for n=6, k=3 is n-3; so k=3 is always possible. And k=0,1 always possible. What about k=n? For n=3, k=3 works; for n=4, we saw impossible; for n=5, impossible; so k=n works only for n=3. So the possible k are 0,1,3, and possibly also n when n=3? That is already included. So maybe the answer is: k can be 0,1,3 for all n≥3, and also k=n when n=3? But the problem asks for a given n, determine all k. So the answer likely depends on n. For n=3, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=4, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=5, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=6, k∈{0,1,3}. So it seems that for all n≥3, k can be 0,1,3. But we need to check if k=3 is always possible. We have a construction: take n-3 lines of one type (say vertical) covering the most points, and then use 3 lines (sunny) to cover the remaining points. For n≥3, we can choose n-3 vertical lines x=1,...,n-3. They cover sum_{i=1}^{n-3} (n+1-i) = sum_{j=4}^{n} j = n(n+1)/2 - 3. The remaining points are those with x≥ n-2. There are 3 such columns? Actually x=n-2, x=n-1, x=n. For x=n-2, b can be 1..? sum≤n+1 => b≤n+1-(n-2)=3, so points (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3). For x=n-1, b≤2: (n-1,1),(n-1,2). For x=n, b≤1: (n,1). That's 6 points. These 6 points can be covered by 3 sunny lines? We need to find 3 sunny lines that cover exactly these 6 points (or at least cover them, and maybe also cover some other points, but we need exactly 3 sunny lines; they can also cover points already covered by vertical lines, but we need the union to be all points). So we can try to cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines that are disjoint from each other and from the vertical lines? But they can also cover some of the vertical points, but we don't need to. However, the vertical lines already cover all points with x≤n-3. So the sunny lines must cover the remaining points (the 6 points). They can also cover some of the vertical points, but that would be extra coverage, which is fine. So we need to find 3 sunny lines whose union contains these 6 points. For n=4, the 6 points are (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). We already have a covering: L1: y=x? That covers (2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) not in set; L2: y=x-1? covers (2,1),(3,2); L3: y=x-2? covers (3,1),(4,2) but (4,2) not in set; so not. But we can use lines: y=x+? Actually we need to cover these 6 points. For n=4, we already gave a construction with 3 sunny lines: (2,1)-(3,2) slope1, (2,2)-(4,1) slope -1/2, (2,3)-(3,1) slope -2. Those are sunny lines, and they cover all 6 points. So that works for n=4. For n=5, the 6 points are (3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Can we cover these with 3 sunny lines? Possibly use lines: y=x? That gives (3,3),(4,4) not, (5,5) no. So not. But we can use: line through (3,1) and (4,2) slope1, covers (3,1),(4,2). line through (3,2) and (5,1) slope? (1-2)/(5-3)= -1/2, covers (3,2),(5,1). line through (3,3) and (4,1) slope -2, covers (3,3),(4,1). That's three lines covering all 6 points. So works. For n=6, the 6 points are (4,1),(4,2),(4,3),(5,1),(5,2),(6,1). Use lines: (4,1)-(5,2) slope1, (4,2)-(6,1) slope -1/2, (4,3)-(5,1) slope -2. So works. So indeed for any n≥3, we can take the vertical lines x=1,...,n-3 (non-sunny) and then the three sunny lines: L1 through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) (slope1); L2 through (n-2,2) and (n,1) (slope -1/2); L3 through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) (slope -2). These are all sunny (slopes 1, -1/2, -2) and they cover all points with x=n-2,n-1,n. So total lines = (n-3) non-sunny + 3 sunny = n. And they cover all points. So k=3 works for all n≥3.

Now, can we get k=2? We need to show impossible. We already argued that with k=2, we need s=n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. So leftover points are at least 3. Those leftover points are the points not on the chosen lines. Since we have freedom to choose the lines, we can try to make the leftover set exactly 4 points, which might be coverable by 2 sunny lines. But we need to check if it's possible to cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The key is to show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines fails. Perhaps we can use a parity or combinatorial argument.

Consider the set of points P. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They are "lines" in the grid. The sunny lines are other slopes. We can think of the "sum of coordinates" s = a+b. The points are partitioned into layers by s. The non-sunny lines are either horizontal (b constant), vertical (a constant), or slope -1 (s constant). The sunny lines are not of these types. We can try to count the number of points on each line.

Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines L1 and L2. Each sunny line can contain at most? For n, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). For n≥3, that's at least 2. But we need to cover the points in P. The non-sunny lines will cover many points. We need to see if it's possible to cover all points with only 2 sunny lines.

Let's try to find a necessary condition: The points in P that are not on any non-sunny line must be covered by the sunny lines. Since the sunny lines are only 2, they can cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points (if each has maximum). But we need to cover all points not on non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most? They are n-2 lines. The maximum union they can cover is N-3 (as computed). So the number of points not on non-sunny lines is at least 3. But we can choose the non-sunny lines to be such that they cover more than N-3? Actually the maximum is N-3, so we cannot cover more than N-3 points with n-2 non-sunny lines. So at least 3 points are left uncovered by non-sunny lines. Those 3 points must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. However, can 2 sunny lines cover 3 points? Yes, if the 3 points are collinear on one sunny line, then that line covers them, and the other sunny line is redundant. But the 3 points are the points not on the non-sunny lines. Could those 3 points be collinear with a sunny slope? The points that are left are the points with x≥ something? In the maximal union case, the 3 leftover points are always (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) (the points with sum=2,3). Are these three collinear? They are not collinear. But we are not forced to use the maximal union; we could use non-sunny lines that are not all disjoint, leaving a different set of points. So we need to see if there exists any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement (points not on these lines) is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement must be a set that is a union of two sunny collinear sets. That complement could have size 4,5,... up to something. Since the non-sunny lines cover at most N-3 points, the complement size is at least 3. So we need to check if we can have complement size 4 (or 5, etc.) and also be coverable by 2 sunny lines. The complement points will be from the triangular grid. Let's try to see if we can have complement of 4 points that are a union of two sunny lines. For n=5, we tried several such sets and didn't find a covering with 3 non-sunny lines. Maybe it's impossible in general.

We can attempt a proof by contradiction: Assume there exists a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Consider the set of points P. Let the two sunny lines be L1 and L2. They cover some points. The non-sunny lines cover the rest. Now, consider the "boundary" of P: the points with a=1 or b=1 or a+b=n+1. Perhaps we can show that the sunny lines cannot cover all boundary points, or something.

Another approach: Use a double counting of the number of points on each type of line. For each line, the number of points it contains is known. The sum of the sizes of the lines (with multiplicities) is the total number of incidences. If we have n lines, the total incidences is at most n * max points per line. But we can also count the number of points that are covered by at least one line, which is N. For a covering, the sum of sizes of lines minus the sum of overlaps equals N. So we have N = sum_{i=1}^n |A_i| - sum_{i<j} |A_i ∩ A_j| + ... This is a combinatorial identity.

For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines have sizes that are at most n. The sum of sizes of non-sunny lines is at most (n-2)*n? Actually the maximum size for a non-sunny line is n (for x+y=n+1). But we don't know the parameters. The sum of sizes of all lines is at least N because each point is on at least one line. But we can use the fact that the sunny lines are "generic" and may intersect each other in at most 1 point, and intersect non-sunny lines in at most something.

Perhaps we can consider the set of points with a=1. These points are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). They lie on the vertical line x=1. If we use non-sunny lines, we could cover some of them. If we use a sunny line, it can contain at most 1 of these points? Actually a sunny line can contain at most one point from x=1 because if it contains two points with x=1, they would be vertical (since same x), and the line would be vertical, not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from the column x=1. Similarly, a sunny line can contain at most one point from the row y=1. Also, a sunny line can contain at most one point from the diagonal x+y = constant? Actually if a sunny line contains two points with the same sum, then the line would have slope -1? Not necessarily: two points with same sum lie on the line x+y = constant, which has slope -1. So a sunny line cannot contain two points with the same sum, because that would imply it is the same line x+y=constant, which is non-sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each sum layer. This is important.

Now, consider the points with a=1. There are n such points. They must be covered by the lines. The non-sunny lines can cover some of them. In particular, the vertical line x=1 (non-sunny) can cover all n points. But if we use x=1, we have one non-sunny line covering all column x=1. Then the other n-3 non-sunny lines must cover the rest. Could we use x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines? Possibly. But we also have the sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point from x=1. So if we have 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from x=1. The remaining n-2 points from x=1 must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be of types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=j covers (1,j) only if it also covers that point; but it can cover at most one point from x=1 because different j give different points. So a horizontal line can cover at most one point from x=1. Similarly, a slope -1 line x+y=c covers at most one point from x=1 (if c=1+j). So each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point from x=1. So to cover n-2 points from x=1 (if sunny lines cover 2), we need at least n-2 non-sunny lines that cover those points. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total. So it's possible if the non-sunny lines are all dedicated to covering the x=1 points. But then they would also cover other points, but we need to cover all points. So if we use x=1 as one non-sunny line, it covers all n points from x=1, so we don't need other non-sunny lines for that column. That would be a better strategy: use vertical line x=1 (non-sunny) to cover all column x=1. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left to cover the rest. Then the sunny lines can cover some points from x=1? They already covered by x=1, so no need. So the sunny lines can cover points from other columns. So this is a plausible construction.

For k=2, we can try: non-sunny lines: x=1, and then choose n-3 non-sunny lines that are all slope -1 lines? Or vertical lines? We need to cover all points. The points with x=1 are covered. The other points are those with x≥2. There are N - n = n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2 points. For n=5, that's 10 points. We need to cover these with n-3=2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. So total lines for the rest: 4 lines. We need to cover 10 points. The maximum union for 2 non-sunny lines is? If we take two disjoint lines of type slope -1, they can cover at most 5+4=9 points (for n=5, sums 6 and 5 cover 5+4=9 points). Then we have 2 sunny lines to cover the remaining 1 point? Actually we need to cover 10 points. If two non-sunny lines cover 9 points, then 1 point left, which can be covered by 1 sunny line (the other sunny line is redundant). But we need exactly 2 sunny lines; we could use both, but one is enough. So we can try to see if we can cover the 10 points with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. For n=5, the 10 points (x≥2) are: (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). That's 4+3+2+1=10. Can we cover these with 2 non-sunny lines? For instance, take x+y=6 (5 points: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)) but (1,5) is not in our set (x=1), so covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 4 points. x+y=5 (4 points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)) covers (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) =3 points. Union =7 points. Missing (2,1),(2,2),(3,1),(4,2?) actually (4,2) is covered by x+y=6, (2,1) not, (2,2) not, (3,1) not. So 3 points missing. Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover those 3 points. That might be possible if those 3 points are collinear? They are (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Are they collinear? (2,1)-(2,2) vertical; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1. So no. So that fails.

What about taking x=2 (vertical) and y=2 (horizontal)? x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,4) =4 points; y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2) but (1,2) not in set, (2,2) yes, (3,2) yes, (4,2) yes ->3 points; union =4+3=7 points (since (2,2) common). Missing (3,1),(3,3),(4,1),(5,1). That's 4 points. Need 2 sunny lines to cover these 4 points. Could they be covered by 2 sunny lines? The points: (3,1),(3,3),(4,1),(5,1). Check slopes: (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (3,3)-(5,1) slope (1-3)/(5-3)=-2/2=-1 non-sunny; (3,1)-(5,1) horizontal; (3,3)-(4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-3)=-2 sunny; (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal; (3,1)-(3,3) vertical. So only sunny pair is (3,3)-(4,1) slope -2. That covers two points, leaves (3,1) and (5,1). Can we cover them with a sunny line? (3,1)-(5,1) horizontal, not sunny. So no.

Thus for n=5, k=2 fails with this attempt. We could try other choices of non-sunny lines. Perhaps we can use a mix: one slope -1 line and one vertical line, etc. But given the pattern, it's plausible that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

I recall a known result: For such a problem, the possible k are 0,1,3, and maybe also n when n=3? But the answer might be: k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1 and n? For n=3, n-1=2 is missing, n=3 is present. So that's not consistent.

Alternatively, maybe the answer is that k can be 0,1,3, and also all integers k such that k ≥ 2 and k ≡ 1 (mod something)? Not.

Given the construction for k=3, and the trivial ones for k=0,1, and the impossibility for k=2, maybe for n≥4, the only possible k are 0,1,3? But for n=3, k=3 is also possible, and k=2 impossible. So the answer: all k in {0,1,3} for n≥3, and additionally k=n when n=3? But 3 is already in {0,1,3}. So it's just {0,1,3} for all n≥3. But we need to confirm that k=3 is always possible for n≥3, which we have. And k=0,1 always possible. So the set of possible k is {0,1,3}. But is k=2 possible for some n? Let's test n=7 quickly with a systematic approach. We need to see if we can cover the 28 points with 2 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines. Thus the only way to get union=24 is to have 5 disjoint lines with total size 24. The largest total size for 5 disjoint lines is 25, so we need to reduce by 1. So it's tricky.

But the key is: we have n-2 non-sunny lines. They are lines in the grid. The complement (points not on these lines) must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. So complement set S = {(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(1,2),(2,3)} cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines? Actually it is covered by y=x and y=x+1, which are sunny. Given the difficulty, it's likely that the only possible k are 0,1,3. But we need to verify that k=2 is indeed impossible for all n≥3. We can attempt a proof by considering the points with a=1 or b=1. Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines. They can contain at most 1 point from each of the columns x=1 and x=n? Actually from the set of points with a=1, there are n points. Each sunny line can contain at most 1 of them. So the 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from that column. The remaining n-2 points from that column must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most 1 point from that column each (since a non-sunny line is either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1; if it is vertical x=1, it covers all n points, so then we don't need other lines for that column. So we can use x=1 as one non-sunny line. That would cover all n points from that column, so then the sunny lines can cover points from other columns. So that's a strategy. So we can assume we have a non-sunny line covering the entire column x=1. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left to cover the rest. Then the sunny lines are free to cover points from x≥2. So the problem reduces to covering the points with x≥2 using n-3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines.

Now, consider the points with a=2. There are 3 points? Actually for a=2, b can be 1..n-1? But sum≤n+1 => b≤n-1, so there are n-1 points? Wait for n, points with a=2 are (2,1),(2,2),...,(2,n-1). That's n-1 points. They are all in the set (x≥2). These points must be covered. The non-sunny lines can cover some of them. In particular, the vertical line x=2 (non-sunny) covers all n-1 points. But if we use x=2, that's one non-sunny line, leaving n-4 non-sunny lines. Then the sunny lines can cover some points from other columns. So we can continue. In fact, we could use all vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-2 as non-sunny lines. That gives n-2 non-sunny lines covering all points with x≤n-2. Then the remaining points are those with x=n-1 and x=n. There are 3 columns? Actually x=n-1 has b≤2, so 2 points; x=n has b≤1, so 1 point. That's 3 points. They must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. But 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any line through two of them is non-sunny. So that fails. So we cannot use all vertical lines up to n-2.

What about using a mix of vertical and slope -1 lines? Perhaps we can use some slope -1 lines to cover the points with larger x? For example, take the slope -1 line x+y=n+1 (the diagonal) which covers n points. That's one non-sunny line. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. The points on that diagonal are covered. The remaining points are those with sum ≤ n. We need to cover them. The points with a=1 and sum ≤ n? Actually the points with a=1 are on the diagonal? (1,n) is on x+y=n+1, yes. So if we use the main diagonal, we cover (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). Then the points not on this diagonal are the ones with sum ≤ n. There are N - n = n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2 points. We need to cover these with n-3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. For n=5, that's 10 points. Could we cover them with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines? Possibly. Let's try: n=5, diagonal x+y=6 covers 5 points: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). The rest are 10 points: (1,1)-(1,4); (2,1)-(2,3); (3,1)-(3,2); (4,1); (5,1)? Actually (5,1) already covered; so (4,1) is not covered? Wait list all points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). Diagonal: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So uncovered: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4); (2,1),(2,2),(2,3); (3,1),(3,2); (4,1). That's 4+3+2+1=10 points. Now we need to cover these 10 with n-3=2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could be, say, x=1 (covers 5 points: (1,1)-(1,5) but (1,5) is diagonal, so it's already covered, but that's okay) and y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) but (5,1) is diagonal, so covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)). So the union of x=1 and y=1 covers: from x=1: (1,1)-(1,5) (5 points); from y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) but (5,1) is diagonal, (1,1) already. So adds (2,1),(3,1),(4,1). So total 5+3=8 points. Missing (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) maybe? Actually we have (1,2),(1,3),(1,4) from x=1; (2,1),(3,1),(4,1) from y=1; but we also need (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). So 3 points missing. Then we need to cover these 3 points with the 2 sunny lines. Can we cover (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) with 2 sunny lines? Possibly using lines through them? For example, line through (2,2) and (2,3) is vertical x=2, non-sunny. Line through (2,2) and (3,2) is horizontal y=2, non-sunny. Line through (2,3) and (3,2) is slope -1, non-sunny. So no sunny line contains two of them. So each sunny line can cover at most 1, need 3 sunny lines. So fails. Could we choose different non-sunny lines? For instance, use x=1 and x=2? x=1 covers 5 points; x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,4) 4 points but (2,4) is diagonal, so adds (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). Union = 5+3=8 points (since (2,1) not in x=1? Actually x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,5); x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,4); but (2,1) is new; (2,2) new; (2,3) new; (2,4) already diagonal; so adds 3. So union=8. Missing (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 6 points. Need to cover with 2 sunny lines? That's impossible. So not.

Thus for n=5, k=2 fails. So it's likely that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

We can try to prove that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the number of points not on non-sunny lines is at least 3 and cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we need a rigorous proof.

Idea: Consider the set of points with a=1 and b=1? Actually maybe use a combinatorial parity argument based on the "sum of coordinates" modulo something. For each line, consider the number of points on it that are in P. For non-sunny lines, the number of points on a line of type horizontal y=j is n+1-j (if j≤n). For vertical x=i, it's n+1-i. For slope -1 x+y=c, it's c-1. For sunny lines, the number of points on the line is at most floor((n+1)/2). But we can also consider the "degree" of each point. Perhaps we can use an averaging argument: The total number of incidences (points on lines) is N = sum of sizes of lines. For n lines, the sum of sizes is at most n * max points per line. But we also have that the sum of sizes is at least N (each point on at least one line). So we have N ≤ sum ≤ n * max. This gives N ≤ n * floor((n+1)/2) = n * floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, N=15, n*floor(6/2)=5*3=15, equality. So if we have 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines, the maximum sum of sizes is 3*n + 2*floor((n+1)/2) = 3n + 2*floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 15+6=21, which is >15. So the sum is not a limiting factor.

Maybe we can use a "covering number" argument: The points P form a "convex" set in the integer lattice. The non-sunny lines are "grid lines". The sunny lines are "diagonals" of other slopes. I recall a known result: The minimal number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular grid of size n is n, and the only ways to achieve that are using the three families of parallel lines. So any covering with exactly n lines must use lines that are all of one type (vertical, horizontal, or slope -1). Therefore, any covering with n lines that includes a sunny line must have at least one non-sunny line that is not of that type? Wait, the problem is to cover all points with n lines. The minimal number of lines needed is n (since the three families each use n lines). So any covering with n lines is a minimal covering. I think it's known that the only minimal coverings of the triangular grid are the three families of parallel lines. That is, any set of n lines that covers all points must be either all vertical, all horizontal, or all slope -1. If that is true, then any covering with n lines that includes a sunny line would not be possible because the sunny line is not of those three types. But we have a construction for k=3 (sunny lines) using n-3 vertical lines and 3 sunny lines. That's a covering with n lines, but it includes both non-sunny and sunny lines. Is that a minimal covering? It uses n lines, and it covers all points. So it is a covering with n lines, but it's not all of one type. So the statement that the only minimal coverings are the three families is false. For n=4, we had a covering with 4 lines: x=1 (non-sunny), and three sunny lines. That's 4 lines covering all points. That's a minimal covering because we cannot cover with fewer than 4 lines (since there are 10 points and each line can cover at most? Actually for n=4, max sunny points=2, so 4 lines could cover up to 8, but we have 10 points; it's possible that a covering with 3 lines exists? For n=4, we already saw that 3 lines cannot cover all points because each line has at most 4 points? Actually we have a covering with 4 lines, so the minimal number is at most 4. Could it be 3? For n=4, total points=10. If we use 3 lines, each can cover at most? The maximum points on a line is 4 (x+y=5 gives 4 points). So 3 lines could cover at most 12 points, but they might overlap. The question is: can we cover all 10 points with 3 lines? Possibly using the three families: vertical x=1,2,3 cover 4+3+2=9 points, leaving 1 point; but we need 3 lines, we could use x=1,2,4? That's 4+3+1=8. So not. Using slope -1 lines: x+y=5 (4), x+y=4 (3), x+y=3 (2) total 9, leaving 1. So not. Using a combination: maybe one vertical, one horizontal, one diagonal? Could cover 4+4+4- overlaps? Possibly 10 if they are disjoint? But they cannot be disjoint because vertical and horizontal intersect; the union might be 4+4+4-? Actually if we take x=1 (4 points), y=1 (4 points), and x+y=4 (3 points), union size: x=1: (1,1)-(1,4) 4; y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) adds (2,1),(3,1),(4,1) but (1,1) already; x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) adds (1,3),(2,2) and (3,1) already; so total =4+3+2=9. So 9 points. Not 10. So 3 lines cannot cover all 10 points. So the minimal number is 4. So our covering with 4 lines is minimal. But it includes sunny lines, so the claim that the only minimal coverings are the three families is false. So that approach doesn't help.

Maybe we can use a "parity" or "mod 2" argument: Consider the set of points with a=1 and b=1. There are 3 points that are "corners". Perhaps we can show that at least 3 lines are needed to cover them, and any sunny line can cover at most 1 of them, so we need at least 3 lines to cover them. So we need to see if it's possible to cover the three points (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) with a combination of n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover these points. For example, the vertical line x=1 covers (1,1) and (1,2). The horizontal line y=1 covers (1,1) and (2,1). The diagonal x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1). So any one of these lines covers 2 of them. So with one non-sunny line, we can cover 2 of them. The remaining 1 can be covered by a sunny line. So it's possible to cover the three corner points with 1 non-sunny and 1 sunny line. But we have 2 sunny lines, so we can cover the three corners with, say, a non-sunny line x=1 covering (1,1),(1,2) and a sunny line through (2,1). That's one sunny line. The other sunny line is redundant for corners. So corners are not an obstacle.

The problem is to cover all points. We need to show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines fails. I'll try a more systematic approach.

Let’s denote the two sunny lines as L1 and L2. For any point P in the triangular grid, its "type" (a,b) is such that a,b≥1 and a+b≤n+1. We can consider the "sum" s = a+b. The non-sunny lines are either horizontal (b constant), vertical (a constant), or slope -1 (s constant). The sunny lines are not of these types.

Now, consider the points with a=1. They are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). They lie on the vertical line x=1. If we use x=1 as a non-sunny line, we cover all of them. So it's beneficial to include x=1. Let's assume we have x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. The points with a=1 are covered. Now, consider the points with a=2. There are n-1 points: (2,1),(2,2),...,(2,n-1). They must be covered. We could use x=2 as a non-sunny line to cover them, but then we would have n-4 non-sunny lines left. If we do that, we have used up to n-2 vertical lines? Actually we can use x=1 and x=2, leaving n-4 non-sunny lines. Then the points with a=3,... are left. As argued, 2 sunny lines cannot cover those 3 points because any line through two of them is non-sunny. So that fails.

Thus we cannot use all vertical lines up to n-2. So we must leave some of the vertical lines uncovered by non-sunny lines, and let the sunny lines cover some points in those columns. For n=5, we attempted to use x=1, x=2, and then two non-sunny lines? Actually we used x=1, and then we have 3 non-sunny lines left (since total non-sunny = n-2=3). In general, we can try to show that with n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover from the triangular grid (with x≥2) is something like (n-2)(n+1)/2 - something? For n, the points are sum ≤ n+1. The largest diagonals are sums n+1, n, ..., 2. Their sizes are n, n-1, ..., 1. For the whole grid, the maximum union with n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3 (since N = n(n+1)/2, and sum of sizes of largest n-2 diagonals is n(n+1)/2 - 3). So that covers N-3 points, leaving 3 points.
