L4: through (1,4) and (2,1) slope -3/2? (1-4)/(2-1)=-3, so slope -3, sunny. That line: through (1,4) and (2,1) slope -3, equation: y-4 = -3(x-1) => y = -3x+7. It covers (1,4) and (2,1). But (2,1) already covered by L3? In L3 we covered (2,1) if we used L3 through (1,3) and (2,1). So overlap. That's okay. So L4 covers (1,4) and (2,1). Then points left: from O: (2,2) covered by L1? L1 covers (2,2); (2,3) not covered; (3,2) not covered; (4,1) not covered; (3,1) covered by L2; (2,1) covered by L3 and L4; (3,2) not covered; (4,1) not covered; also (1,1) covered by L1; (1,2) covered by L2; (1,3) covered by L3; (1,4) covered by L4. So uncovered: (2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 3 points. We have already used 4 lines, but they are all sunny. We need to cover these 3 points. Could we adjust the lines to include them? For instance, if we change L2 to go through (1,2) and (3,2) slope 0? That's horizontal not sunny. Or (1,2) and (4,1) slope -1/3? That would cover (1,2) and (4,1) but then (1,2) is from V, (4,1) is from O. That line: slope -1/3, equation: y-2 = -1/3(x-1) => y = -1/3 x + 7/3. For x=2, y= -2/3+7/3=5/3 not integer; x=3, y=-1+7/3=4/3; x=4, y=-4/3+7/3=1, so (4,1) on it. So that line covers (1,2) and (4,1). Then we need to cover other points. Let's try a different approach.

Maybe we can use a non-sunny line that is not x+y=6 but some other line that covers many points, so that the leftover points are easier to cover with sunny lines. For n=5, the non-sunny line can be x+y=5 (covers 4 points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)), or x+y=4 (covers 3 points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)), or x+y=6 (covers 5 points), or x+y=3 (covers 2 points), etc. The maximum is 5. If we use x+y=5, we cover 4 points, leaving 11 points. That's 11 points to cover with 4 sunny lines. That seems unlikely because each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so max distinct points covered by 4 lines is 12, but we need 11, so possible in principle if lines are nearly disjoint. However, we also need to cover the points on x+y=5? No, they are already covered. So we need to cover the remaining 11 points with 4 sunny lines. Could that be done? Possibly, but we need to check if the structure of the remaining points allows a covering.

Given the difficulty, maybe we need to find a general characterization. Let's step back and think about the problem more systematically.

The points P = {(a,b) in Z^2: a,b >= 1, a+b <= n+1}. We want to cover them with n lines. Each line is either sunny (slope not 0, ∞, -1) or non-sunny (slope 0, ∞, or -1). Let's denote the number of lines of each type. We have n lines total. The problem asks for which k (number of sunny lines) does such a covering exist.

Observations:
- The three families of non-sunny lines (vertical, horizontal, slope -1) each individually cover P with n lines. So k=0 always works.
- For any n, we can take n-1 vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 and one sunny line through (n,1). That covers all points. So k=1 always works.
- For k=n, we need all lines sunny. Is that always possible? For n=3, yes. For n=4, we found impossible because each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, and we need 10 points with 4 lines, but the maximum distinct points covered by 4 lines with at most 2 points each is 8, and we have 10 points, so impossible. For n=5, each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so with 5 lines we could cover at most 15 points, which is exactly the number of points. So we need a partition of the 15 points into 5 collinear triples with sunny slopes. We attempted to find such a partition and argued that it's impossible because the remaining 6 points after using three triples (slope1, slope-1/2, slope-2) could not be covered by two triples, but we need to check if there exists any partition into 5 triples. Perhaps we can use a different set of triples. For n=5, we have points with a+b ≤6. Let's list all possible collinear triples with sunny slopes. For slope 1, points (a,a) for a=1,2,3: that's triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). For slope -1/2, points (a, -a/2 + c) we need integer coordinates. For example, line through (1,3),(3,2),(5,1) slope -1/2: that's triple (1,3),(3,2),(5,1). For slope -2, line through (1,5),(2,3),(3,1): triple (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). For slope 2, points (1,2),(2,4) only 2 points; (3,6) not in P. So no triple. For slope 1/2, points (2,1),(4,2) only 2; (6,3) not. So no triple. For slope -1/3, points (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So no triple. For slope 3, points (1,3) only; (2,6) no. So no triple. For slope -3, points (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So only three triples are possible. Therefore we cannot partition the 15 points into 5 triples. So k=n=5 impossible.

For n=6, points with a+b ≤7, total points = 1+2+3+4+5+6 = 21. Each sunny line can contain at most? For slope 1, points (a,a) for a=1..3? 2a ≤7 => a≤3.5, so a=1,2,3: triple. For slope 2, points (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) sum=8? 3+6=9>7, so only a=1,2: two points. For slope 3, (1,3),(2,6) sum=8? 2+6=8>7, so only (1,3) maybe. For slope 1/2, (2,1),(4,2),(6,3) sum=9? 6+3=9>7, so only (2,1),(4,2) maybe. For slope -1/2, (1,5),(3,4),(5,3),(7,2) but (7,2) sum=9>7, so (1,5),(3,4),(5,3) triple? Check: (1,5): sum=6, (3,4): sum=7, (5,3): sum=8>7, so (5,3) not in P. So only (1,5),(3,4) maybe. For slope -2, (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) triple? (1,5) sum=6, (2,3) sum=5, (3,1) sum=4, all ≤7, yes triple. For slope -3, (1,4),(2,1) only. So maximum points on a sunny line is 3 (slope 1, slope -2, etc.). So with 6 sunny lines, maximum distinct points covered is 18, but we have 21 points. So k=n=6 impossible. In fact, for general n, what is the maximum number of points on a sunny line? A line with slope m (rational, not 0,∞,-1) that passes through many points of P. The points P are in a triangle. For a given rational slope m, the points on the line are those (a,b) with b = m a + c, and a,b integers, a,b>=1, a+b≤n+1. The number of such points is at most something like floor((n+1)/(1+m))? Actually, if m>0, then b = m a + c, and a+b = a + m a + c = (1+m)a + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ (n+1-c)/(1+m). Also a>=1. So the number of integer a is about (n+1)/(1+m). For m=1, we get a ≤ (n+1-c)/2, so max 3 points if c is chosen appropriately (c=1,2,3? For n=5, c=1 gives a≤3, so 3 points; for n=6, c=1 gives a≤3.5, so a=1,2,3 gives 3 points; for n=7, c=1 gives a≤3.5, so 3 points; but c can be 0? Then a≤ (n+1)/(1+m). For m=1, a≤ (n+1)/2, so number of points is floor((n+1)/2). So with n=5, floor(6/2)=3; n=6, floor(7/2)=3; n=7, floor(8/2)=4; etc. So for m=1, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). For m=2, a ≤ (n+1-c)/3, so max points floor((n+1)/3). So the maximum over all sunny slopes is floor((n+1)/2) (since 1 is the smallest positive slope? Actually for slopes that are not -1, we can also have negative slopes. (6,2) sum=8, yes triple. So maximum points on a sunny line is at most something like floor((n+1)/2) for positive slopes? For negative slopes, the number of points could be up to floor((n+1)/2) as well? For slope -1/2, the points are (2k, c - k). So the number of k such that 2k ≥1, and 2k + (c-k) = k + c ≤ n+1. So k ≤ n+1-c. So maximum points is about n+1? But we need integer coordinates; for slope -1/2, the line contains points where x is even, so the maximum number of points is at most (n+1)/2? Because k runs from 1 to something, and the x-coordinate is 2k, so the number of points is at most floor((n+1)/2). Actually for slope -1/2, we can have points (2, c-1), (4, c-2), ..., up to x = 2(n+1-c) if c chosen appropriately? So the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). Because the points are in a triangle of size n, and a line can intersect the triangle in at most n points? Actually, a line can intersect a convex set in a line segment; the integer points on that segment are at most something. For a right triangle, the maximum number of collinear integer points with a given slope is something like floor((n+1)/2) for slopes not 0,∞,-1. In fact, the line x+y = constant gives n points (for c=n+1). So the maximum for non-sunny lines is n. For sunny lines, the maximum is smaller. So for k=n (all sunny), we would need to cover n(n+1)/2 points with n lines, each containing at most floor((n+1)/2) points. So the maximum total points covered (counting multiplicities) is n * floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2, total 6, which matches. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2, total 8, but we have 10 points, so impossible. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3, total 15, matches. But we found that it's impossible to have a partition into 5 triples because the only triples are of certain types and they don't cover all points. So there might be a necessary condition: the points on the line must be collinear with a sunny slope, but we need to cover all points. For n=5, we needed 5 triples, but only 3 possible triples exist. So k=n=5 fails.

Thus we need to find all k for which there exists a covering with exactly k sunny lines. The problem likely expects a characterization like: k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1 and n? Or maybe k can be any integer from 0 to n except n-1? For n=3, we had k=0,1,3. So 2 is missing, which is n-1. For n=4, we had k=0,1,3. So 2 is missing, which is n-2? Actually n=4, n-1=3 is present, n-2=2 is missing, n=4 missing. So pattern: for n=4, k=0,1,3 (3 is n-1). For n=3, k=0,1,3 (3 is n). So it's not simply all except n-1.

Maybe we need to consider the general case. Let's think about the covering problem in terms of "lines" that are either "sunny" or "non-sunny". The points P have a triangular structure. Now, can we get k=2? We already tried for n=3,4,5,6 and seemed impossible. But perhaps for larger n, k=2 might be possible because the leftover points after using 2 non-sunny lines (n-2 lines) might be covered by 2 sunny lines if we choose the non-sunny lines appropriately. So it seems that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to prove it in general.

Let's try to find a necessary condition on k. Perhaps the answer is that k can be 0, 1, and n only? But we have k=3 works for n=4,5,6,... and for n=3, k=3 works (n). For n=4, k=3 works (n-1). For n=5, k=3 works (n-2). For n=6, k=3 works (n-3). So k=3 is always possible. For n=3, k=0,1,3. For n=4, k=0,1,3. For n=5, k=0,1,3. For n=6, we have k=0,1,3? Did we find k=3 for n=6? Wait we used 3 sunny lines to cover those 3 points, but we need exactly k sunny lines, and we have 3 sunny lines. But we also have 3 non-sunny lines, total 6 lines. So k=3 works. For n=6, we have construction: non-sunny lines: x+y=7, x+y=6, x+y=5 (3 lines). They cover 6+5+4=15 points. The leftover points are (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Then we take sunny lines: L1 through (1,2) and (2,2) is horizontal y=2, not sunny. Actually we need sunny lines that cover those points. For the set {(1,2),(1,3),(2,2)} we can take L1: through (1,2) and (2,2) slope 0? Horizontal y=2 non-sunny. L2: through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1 non-sunny. L3: through (1,2) and (1,3) vertical. So no sunny line covers two of them. So we need to cover each point with a sunny line, but then we would have 3 sunny lines, each covering one point. But then the union of the sunny lines covers those 3 points, but each sunny line must be distinct and sunny. So we can take any line through (1,2) that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For example, line through (1,2) and (2,1) has slope -1? (1-2)/(2-1)=-1, non-sunny. Through (1,2) and (2,3) slope 1 sunny; that line passes through (1,2) and (2,3) but (2,3) is already covered by non-sunny? Actually (2,3) is on x+y=5? 2+3=5, yes it's covered. Actually (2-3)/(3-1)=-1/2, sunny. So L2: through (1,3) and (3,2) (which is covered by non-sunny). That's sunny. For (2,2), we need a sunny line through (2,2). These three lines are all sunny and distinct. So k=3 works for n=6. So indeed we have a construction for n=6, k=3.

Now, what about k=4 for n=6? We attempted and failed. For n=6, we need 4 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover up to 11 points (x+y=7 and x+y=6 cover 6+5=11). Then leftover 10 points. Can we cover those 10 points with 4 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points? For n=6, max sunny points is 3 (slope 1 gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3); slope -2 gives (1,5),(2,3),(3,1); etc.) So 4 lines can cover at most 12 points, but we need to cover 10 distinct points. So possible in principle. But we need to check if the leftover points can be partitioned into 4 sunny collinear sets (each of size up to 3). Thus for n=5, we haven't found a configuration for k=2. Maybe it's impossible.

Given the analysis, it seems that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. Let's attempt to prove it generally.

We have n lines. Let s be the number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - s. The total points is N = n(n+1)/2. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They can cover at most some points. We need to cover all points. For k=2, we need s = n-2. The maximum number of distinct points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines is achieved by taking n-2 lines of one type that are pairwise disjoint. For vertical lines, the sizes are n, n-1, ..., 1. So the sum of the largest n-2 numbers is sum_{i=1}^{n-2} (n+1-i) = sum_{j=3}^{n} j = n(n+1)/2 - 3. So maximum union = N - 3. So we can cover at most N - 3 points with n-2 non-sunny lines. Then leftover points are at least 3. But we need to cover those leftover points with 2 sunny lines. However, the leftover points are exactly the points not on the selected lines. We can choose the non-sunny lines in many ways; we are not forced to use the maximal union. For k=2, we need to select n-2 non-sunny lines such that the union size is at most N (obviously). We need the union to be N minus the number of points that will be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines will cover the complement. So we need to cover some set of points (the complement) with 2 sunny lines. That complement must be a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. So we need to find a set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement is coverable by 2 sunny lines. Since we have freedom to choose the non-sunny lines, we can try to make the complement small (like 4 points) and also coverable.

But note: The total number of lines is n. If we use n-2 non-sunny lines, we have 2 sunny lines left. So we need to cover all points in P. The non-sunny lines cover some points. The sunny lines must cover the remaining points. So we need to find a selection of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the remaining points (the ones not on these lines) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That is the problem.

We attempted for n=5 to find such a selection. We found one that gave complement of 4 points, but those 4 points were not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could there be a selection that gives complement of 3 points? Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points, which is impossible because each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them (since any line through two of them is non-sunny). So complement cannot be 3. Could it be 4? Then we need to check if any set of 4 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That is possible if the 4 points can be partitioned into two sunny collinear sets. For example, a set like {(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4)} can be covered by 1 sunny line (y=x). That's 1 line, but we have 2 lines; we could also cover it with 2 lines (e.g., y=x and y=x-2). So any set that is a union of two sunny lines works. So we need to find if there exists a set of 4 points that is a union of two sunny lines, and also such that the complement (the other 11 points for n=5) can be covered by 3 non-sunny lines. That is the existence.

We tried several sets of 4 points that are unions of two sunny lines, e.g., points on y=x and y=x-2. For n=5, the set of points on y=x with sum≤6: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum=6 is in P, so triple. On y=x-2: points (3,1),(4,2) but (5,3) sum=8>6, so only two points. So the union would be {(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(3,1),(4,2)}? That's 5 points, not 4. To get exactly 4 points, we need a set of 4 points that is a union of two sunny lines, each with at most 2 points? Actually if each line has 2 points, union size 4. For n=5, can we find two sunny lines each containing exactly 2 points from P such that the lines are distinct and the union is a set of 4 points? For instance, line y=x+1: points (1,2),(2,3) are in P (sums 3,5). Line y=x-1: points (2,1),(3,2) are in P (sums 3,5). Their union is {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2)} which are 4 points. Are these points coverable by 2 sunny lines? Yes, the lines themselves are sunny. So that set works. Can we cover the complement (the other 11 points) with 3 non-sunny lines? The complement would be all points except these 4. Points for n=5: list them. The set we want to leave uncovered is S = {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2)}. The other points: (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5); (2,4); (3,1),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). That's 1+2+1+2+1+1+1 =? Let's list all points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5); (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). So total 15. Remove S: (1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2) are removed. We need to find 3 lines (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) whose union is exactly these 11 points. The maximum union for 3 lines is 12 (by taking three disjoint lines of one family). Maybe we can use a combination of vertical/horizontal lines. For instance, take x=1, x=2, and y=2? x=1 gives (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) but (1,2) is in S, so we need to cover the set without S; x=1 would cover (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) (4 points). x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4) but (2,1) and (2,3) are in S, so covers (2,2),(2,4) (2 points). y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2) but (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2) etc. (2,2) already in x=2, (1,2) in S, (3,2) in S, (4,2) not in set? Actually (4,2) is in set? Yes (4,2) is in set (from complement). y=2 also includes (5,2) but (5,2) not in set. So y=2 adds (4,2) maybe? Actually (4,2) is already in x=1? No, x=1 covers (1,2) only; (4,2) is not in x=1 or x=2. So y=2 adds (4,2) and maybe (5,2) not. So union = 4+2+1=7. Not enough. So not.

Thus maybe this complement set S cannot be covered by 3 non-sunny lines. So we need to try other S of 4 points that are unions of two sunny lines. For n=5, the possible unions of two sunny lines that are in P are limited. Let's systematically find all sets of 4 points that are unions of two sunny lines (each line may have 2 points, or one line with 3 and the other with 1, but then union size could be 4). The only sunny lines that contain points from P are those with slopes that are not 0,∞,-1. For n=5, the points are sum≤6. Possible sunny lines: 
- y=x: points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points.
- y=x+1: (1,2),(2,3) -> 2 points.
- y=x+2: (1,3) -> 1 point.
- y=x-1: (2,1),(3,2) -> 2 points.
- y=x-2: (3,1) -> 1 point.
- y=2x: (1,2),(2,4) -> 2 points.
- y=2x+1: (1,3) -> 1 point.
- y=2x-1: (2,3) -> 1 point.
- y=3x: (1,3) -> 1 point.
- y=3x+2: (1,5) -> 1 point.
- y=4x: (1,4) -> 1 point.
- y=5x: (1,5) -> 1 point.
- y=6x: (1,6) not in P.
- y=-x+? negative slopes: y=-2x+7: (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) -> 3 points.
- y=-2x+6: (1,4),(2,2),(3,0) no ->2 points.
- y=-2x+5: (1,3),(2,1) ->2 points.
- y=-3x+8: (1,5),(2,2) ->2 points.
- y=-3x+7: (1,4),(2,1) ->2 points.
- y=-4x+9: (1,5) ->1 point.
- y=-x+? slope -1: not sunny.
- y=-x+3: (1,2),(2,1) ->2 points (non-sunny).
- y=-x+4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) ->3 points (non-sunny).
- y=-x+5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) ->4 points (non-sunny).
- y=-x+6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) ->5 points (non-sunny).

So the sunny lines that have at least 2 points are: y=x (3), y=x+1 (2), y=x-1 (2), y=2x (2), y=2x+1 (1), y=2x-1 (1), y=3x (1), y=3x+2 (1), y=4x (1), y=5x (1), y=-2x+7 (3), y=-2x+6 (2), y=-2x+5 (2), y=-3x+8 (2), y=-3x+7 (2), y=-4x+9 (1). So the only lines with 3 points are y=x and y=-2x+7. Also y=-2x+6 has 2 points, etc.

Now, we want a set S of 4 points that is a union of two sunny lines. The possibilities: 
- Two lines each with 2 points, disjoint (union size 4). These pairs can be: (y=x+1 and y=x-1) -> S1 = {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2)}. (y=x-1 and y=2x) -> (2,1),(3,2) and (1,2),(2,4) union = (2,1),(3,2),(1,2),(2,4) =4 points? Check: (2,1) and (3,2) from y=x-1; (1,2) and (2,4) from y=2x; they are disjoint? (y=x+1 and y=-2x+7) -> y=x+1: (1,2),(2,3); y=-2x+7: (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). They share (2,3), union = (1,2),(2,3),(1,5),(3,1) =4 points? - Two lines each with 2 points that overlap by 1 point, giving union size 3, not 4.
- One line with 3 points and one line with 2 points that are disjoint? Then union size 5. So only possibilities for union size 4 are: two lines each with 2 points disjoint, or one line with 3 points and one line with 1 point disjoint. So we have several candidates: S1, S2, S3, S4, S5.

Now, for each, we need to check if the complement (the other 11 points) can be covered by 3 non-sunny lines. We already attempted S1 and it gave complement of 4 points? We attempted a few selections but didn't find a covering. Could there be a covering? Let's try to see if we can cover those 11 points with 3 lines. Wait (1,3) is there, (1,4) there, (1,5) there, (1,1) there. So all points are covered. But we also have (1,2) is not in complement, so it's fine. So the union of x=1, x+y=6, and y=2 covers exactly the complement? Let's list all points in complement: (1,1),(1,3),(1,4); (2,2),(2,4); (3,1),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). That's 4+2+2+2+1 = 11. From our union: x=1 gives (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) but (1,2) is not in complement, but it's covered anyway. So the union includes (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) (4 points). x+y=6 gives (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So adds (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) (4 new). y=2 gives (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2). (1,2) already in x=1; (4,2) already in x+y=6; (2,2) new; (3,2) not in complement; (5,2) not. So adds (2,2). So union = (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1),(2,2). That's 10 points. We are missing (3,1) and (4,1) and (1,?) Actually (3,1) and (4,1) are not covered. So we still have 2 points missing. So not good.

Thus we need to find a covering of the complement with 3 non-sunny lines. Since we have many points, it's likely that it's impossible. Given the difficulty, it's plausible that for n=5, k=2 is impossible. So the pattern: possible k are 0,1, and 3? But for n=3, k=3 works; n=4, k=3 works; n=5, k=3 works? We need to check if k=3 works for n=5. We already gave a construction: non-sunny lines: x+y=6, x+y=5, x+y=4? That's 3 lines, covering 5+4+3=12 points, leaving 3 points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points? But we have 2 sunny lines, and we need to cover 3 points; as before, each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them, so we need 3 sunny lines. So k=3 would require 3 sunny lines, but we only have 2 sunny lines if we use 3 non-sunny lines. So that's not a construction for k=3. For n=5, we need exactly 3 sunny lines. So we need to use 2 non-sunny lines. For n=5, total points=15, we need 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most? For n=5, using 2 non-sunny lines, the maximum union is 5+4=9 points (by taking two disjoint lines of one family). Then leftover 6 points. Those 6 points must be covered by 3 sunny lines. As we argued, there exist sunny lines that cover 3 points? For n=5, the only lines with 3 points are y=x (3 points) and y=-2x+7 (3 points). So if we can cover the 6 leftover points with these two lines? But they would be two lines, not three. We need three sunny lines. So we need to partition the 6 points into three sets each covered by a sunny line. Since there is no triple among the points? Possibly there is a triple if the leftover points are, say, the points on y=x (3 points) and points on y=x+1 (2 points) and y=x-1 (2 points) etc. But we need exactly three sunny lines that together cover the six points. Could we use lines that each cover 2 points? That would be three lines each with 2 points, disjoint. Is there a set of 6 points that can be covered by three sunny lines each with 2 points? For example, take S = {(1,2),(2,3),(2,1),(3,2),(3,1),(4,1)}? That's 6 points, but are they coverable by three sunny lines? Possibly lines: y=x+1: (1,2),(2,3); y=x-1: (2,1),(3,2); y=2x-1: (3,5) no; y=-2x+7: (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) not disjoint. So maybe not.

Given the time, it's more efficient to look for a general result. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n≥3 be an integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both: for all positive integers a,b with a+b≤n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." The answer might be: k can be 0,1,3, and maybe also n? But for n=3, k=3 is n; for n=4, k=3 is n-1; for n=5, k=3 is n-2; for n=6, k=3 is n-3; so k=3 is always possible. And k=0,1 always possible. What about k=n? For n=3, k=3 works; for n=4, we saw impossible; for n=5, impossible; so k=n works only for n=3. So the possible k are 0,1,3, and possibly also n when n=3? That is already included. So maybe the answer is: k can be 0,1,3 for all n≥3, and also k=n when n=3? But the problem asks for a given n, determine all k. So the answer likely depends on n. For n=3, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=4, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=5, k∈{0,1,3}. For n=6, k∈{0,1,3}. So it seems that for all n≥3, k can be 0,1,3. But we need to check if k=3 is always possible. We have a construction: take n-3 lines of one type (say vertical) covering the most points, and then use 3 lines (sunny) to cover the remaining points. For n≥3, we can choose n-3 vertical lines x=1,...,n-3. They cover sum_{i=1}^{n-3} (n+1-i) = sum_{j=4}^{n} j = n(n+1)/2 - 3. The remaining points are those with x≥ n-2. There are 3 such columns? Actually x=n-2, x=n-1, x=n. For x=n-2, b can be 1..? sum≤n+1 => b≤n+1-(n-2)=3, so points (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3). For x=n-1, b≤2: (n-1,1),(n-1,2). For x=n, b≤1: (n,1). That's 6 points. These 6 points can be covered by 3 sunny lines? We need to find 3 sunny lines that cover exactly these 6 points (or at least cover them, and maybe also cover some other points, but we need exactly 3 sunny lines; they can also cover points already covered by vertical lines, but we need the union to be all points). So we can try to cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines that are disjoint from each other and from the vertical lines? But they can also cover some of the vertical points, but we don't need to. However, the vertical lines already cover all points with x≤n-3. So the sunny lines must cover the remaining points (the 6 points). They can also cover some of the vertical points, but that would be extra coverage, which is fine. So we need to find 3 sunny lines whose union contains these 6 points. For n=4, the 6 points are (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). We already have a covering: L1: y=x? That covers (2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) not in set; L2: y=x-1? covers (2,1),(3,2); L3: y=x-2? covers (3,1),(4,2) but (4,2) not in set; so not. But we can use lines: y=x+? Actually we need to cover these 6 points. For n=4, we already gave a construction with 3 sunny lines: (2,1)-(3,2) slope1, (2,2)-(4,1) slope -1/2, (2,3)-(3,1) slope -2. Those are sunny lines, and they cover all 6 points. So that works for n=4. For n=5, the 6 points are (3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Can we cover these with 3 sunny lines? Possibly use lines: y=x? That gives (3,3),(4,4) not, (5,5) no. So not. But we can use: line through (3,1) and (4,2) slope1, covers (3,1),(4,2). line through (3,2) and (5,1) slope? (1-2)/(5-3)= -1/2, covers (3,2),(5,1). line through (3,3) and (4,1) slope -2, covers (3,3),(4,1). That's three lines covering all 6 points. So works. For n=6, the 6 points are (4,1),(4,2),(4,3),(5,1),(5,2),(6,1). Use lines: (4,1)-(5,2) slope1, (4,2)-(6,1) slope -1/2, (4,3)-(5,1) slope -2. So works. So indeed for any n≥3, we can take the vertical lines x=1,...,n-3 (non-sunny) and then the three sunny lines: L1 through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) (slope1); L2 through (n-2,2) and (n,1) (slope -1/2); L3 through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) (slope -2). These are all sunny (slopes 1, -1/2, -2) and they cover all points with x=n-2,n-1,n. So total lines = (n-3) non-sunny + 3 sunny = n. And they cover all points. So k=3 works for all n≥3.

Now, can we get k=2? We need to show impossible. We already argued that with k=2, we need s=n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. So leftover points are at least 3. Those leftover points are the points not on the chosen lines. Since we have freedom to choose the lines, we can try to make the leftover set exactly 4 points, which might be coverable by 2 sunny lines. But we need to check if it's possible to cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The key is to show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines fails. Perhaps we can use a parity or combinatorial argument.

Consider the set of points P. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They are "lines" in the grid. The sunny lines are other slopes. We can think of the "sum of coordinates" s = a+b. The points are partitioned into layers by s. The non-sunny lines are either horizontal (b constant), vertical (a constant), or slope -1 (s constant). The sunny lines are not of these types. We can try to count the number of points on each line.

Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines L1 and L2. Each sunny line can contain at most? For n, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). For n≥3, that's at least 2. But we need to cover the points in P. The non-sunny lines will cover many points. We need to see if it's possible to cover all points with only 2 sunny lines.

Let's try to find a necessary condition: The points in P that are not on any non-sunny line must be covered by the sunny lines. Since the sunny lines are only 2, they can cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points (if each has maximum). But we need to cover all points not on non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most? They are n-2 lines. The maximum union they can cover is N-3 (as computed). So the number of points not on non-sunny lines is at least 3. But we can choose the non-sunny lines to be such that they cover more than N-3? Actually the maximum is N-3, so we cannot cover more than N-3 points with n-2 non-sunny lines. So at least 3 points are left uncovered by non-sunny lines. Those 3 points must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. However, can 2 sunny lines cover 3 points? Yes, if the 3 points are collinear on one sunny line, then that line covers them, and the other sunny line is redundant. But the 3 points are the points not on the non-sunny lines. Could those 3 points be collinear with a sunny slope? The points that are left are the points with x≥ something? In the maximal union case, the 3 leftover points are always (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) (the points with sum=2,3). Are these three collinear? They are not collinear. But we are not forced to use the maximal union; we could use non-sunny lines that are not all disjoint, leaving a different set of points. So we need to see if there exists any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement (points not on these lines) is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement must be a set that is a union of two sunny collinear sets. That complement could have size 4,5,... up to something. Since the non-sunny lines cover at most N-3 points, the complement size is at least 3. So we need to check if we can have complement size 4 (or 5, etc.) and also be coverable by 2 sunny lines. The complement points will be from the triangular grid. Let's try to see if we can have complement of 4 points that are a union of two sunny lines. For n=5, we tried several such sets and didn't find a covering with 3 non-sunny lines. Maybe it's impossible in general.

We can attempt a proof by contradiction: Assume there exists a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Consider the set of points P. Let the two sunny lines be L1 and L2. They cover some points. The non-sunny lines cover the rest. Now, consider the "boundary" of P: the points with a=1 or b=1 or a+b=n+1. Perhaps we can show that the sunny lines cannot cover all boundary points, or something.

Another approach: Use a double counting of the number of points on each type of line. For each line, the number of points it contains is known. The sum of the sizes of the lines (with multiplicities) is the total number of incidences. If we have n lines, the total incidences is at most n * max points per line. But we can also count the number of points that are covered by at least one line, which is N. For a covering, the sum of sizes of lines minus the sum of overlaps equals N. So we have N = sum_{i=1}^n |A_i| - sum_{i<j} |A_i ∩ A_j| + ... This is a combinatorial identity.

For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines have sizes that are at most n. The sum of sizes of non-sunny lines is at most (n-2)*n? Actually the maximum size for a non-sunny line is n (for x+y=n+1). But we don't know the parameters. The sum of sizes of all lines is at least N because each point is on at least one line. But we can use the fact that the sunny lines are "generic" and may intersect each other in at most 1 point, and intersect non-sunny lines in at most something.

Perhaps we can consider the set of points with a=1. These points are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). They lie on the vertical line x=1. If we use non-sunny lines, we could cover some of them. If we use a sunny line, it can contain at most 1 of these points? Actually a sunny line can contain at most one point from x=1 because if it contains two points with x=1, they would be vertical (since same x), and the line would be vertical, not sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from the column x=1. Similarly, a sunny line can contain at most one point from the row y=1. Also, a sunny line can contain at most one point from the diagonal x+y = constant? Actually if a sunny line contains two points with the same sum, then the line would have slope -1? Not necessarily: two points with same sum lie on the line x+y = constant, which has slope -1. So a sunny line cannot contain two points with the same sum, because that would imply it is the same line x+y=constant, which is non-sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each sum layer. This is important.

Now, consider the points with a=1. There are n such points. They must be covered by the lines. The non-sunny lines can cover some of them. In particular, the vertical line x=1 (non-sunny) can cover all n points. But if we use x=1, we have one non-sunny line covering all column x=1. Then the other n-3 non-sunny lines must cover the rest. Could we use x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines? Possibly. But we also have the sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point from x=1. So if we have 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from x=1. The remaining n-2 points from x=1 must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be of types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=j covers (1,j) only if it also covers that point; but it can cover at most one point from x=1 because different j give different points. So a horizontal line can cover at most one point from x=1. Similarly, a slope -1 line x+y=c covers at most one point from x=1 (if c=1+j). So each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point from x=1. We can try to prove that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the number of points not on non-sunny lines is at least 3 and cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we need a rigorous proof.

Idea: Consider the set of points with a=1 and b=1? Actually maybe use a combinatorial parity argument based on the "sum of coordinates" modulo something. Perhaps we can use an averaging argument: The total number of incidences (points on lines) is N = sum of sizes of lines. For n lines, the sum of sizes is at most n * max points per line. But we also have that the sum of sizes is at least N (each point on at least one line). So we have N ≤ sum ≤ n * max. This gives N ≤ n * floor((n+1)/2) = n * floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, N=15, n*floor(6/2)=5*3=15, equality. So if we have 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines, the maximum sum of sizes is 3*n + 2*floor((n+1)/2) = 3n + 2*floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 15+6=21, which is >15. So the sum is not a limiting factor.

Maybe we can use a "covering number" argument: The points P form a "convex" set in the integer lattice. The non-sunny lines are "grid lines". The sunny lines are "diagonals" of other slopes. I recall a known result: The minimal number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular grid of size n is n, and the only ways to achieve that are using the three families of parallel lines. So any covering with exactly n lines must use lines that are all of one type (vertical, horizontal, or slope -1). The problem is to cover all points. We need to show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines fails. I'll try a more systematic approach.

Let’s denote the two sunny lines as L1 and L2. For any point P in the triangular grid, its "type" (a,b) is such that a,b≥1 and a+b≤n+1. We can consider the "sum" s = a+b. The non-sunny lines are either horizontal (b constant), vertical (a constant), or slope -1 (s constant). The sunny lines are not of these types.

Now, consider the points with a=1. They are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). They lie on the vertical line x=1. If we use x=1 as a non-sunny line, we cover all of them. So it's beneficial to include x=1. Let's assume we have x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. The points with a=1 are covered. Now, consider the points with a=2. There are n-1 points: (2,1),(2,2),...,(2,n-1). They must be covered. We could use x=2 as a non-sunny line to cover them, but then we would have n-4 non-sunny lines left. If we do that, we have used up to n-2 vertical lines? Actually we can use x=1 and x=2, leaving n-4 non-sunny lines. Then the points with a=3,... are left. If we continue using all vertical lines up to x=n-2, we would use n-2 vertical lines, covering all points with x≤n-2. Then we have 0 non-sunny lines left? Actually we have n-2 non-sunny lines total, so if we use x=1,...,n-2, that's n-2 non-sunny lines. Then we have 2 sunny lines left to cover the points with x=n-1 and x=n. Those are 3 points. As argued, 2 sunny lines cannot cover those 3 points because any line through two of them is non-sunny. So that fails.

Thus we cannot use all vertical lines up to n-2. So we must leave some of the vertical lines uncovered by non-sunny lines, and let the sunny lines cover some points in those columns. For n=5, we attempted to use x=1, x=2, and then two non-sunny lines? Actually we used x=1, and then we have 3 non-sunny lines left (since total non-sunny = n-2=3). So we could use x=1 and then choose two more non-sunny lines (maybe slope -1 lines) to cover the rest. So we need to see if there exists a set of 3 non-sunny lines (including x=1) and 2 sunny lines that cover all points. That is equivalent to: cover the points with x≥2 using 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines.

For n=5, the points with x≥2 are 10 points. We need to cover these with 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The maximum union for 2 non-sunny lines is 5+4=9 (if we take two disjoint lines of one type). Then we have 1 point left, which can be covered by 1 sunny line. But we have 2 sunny lines, so we can cover that point with one sunny line and the other sunny line is redundant. So the only way to cover 10 points with 2 non-sunny and 2 sunny lines is if the 2 non-sunny lines cover 9 points, leaving 1 point, and the 2 sunny lines cover that 1 point (one of them covers it, the other is extra). That is possible. So we need to find 2 non-sunny lines that cover 9 points from the 10 points (with x≥2). Is that possible? The 10 points are: (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). The maximum union for 2 non-sunny lines can be 9 if we take, say, x+y=6 (covers 4 points: (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)) and x+y=5 (covers 3 points: (2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). Their union is 4+3=7 points, not 9. Could we take x=2 and y=2? x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,4)=4 points; y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2) but (1,2) not in set, (2,2) yes, (3,2) yes, (4,2) yes, (5,2) not in set. So union=4+3=7 (since (2,2) common). Not 9. x=2 and x=3? x=2:4 points; x=3: (3,1)-(3,3)=3 points; union=7 (no overlap). Not 9. x=2 and x+y=6? x=2:4 points; x+y=6: (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) ->4 points, overlap (2,4) -> union=4+4-1=7. Not 9. So no two non-sunny lines can cover 9 points from this set. The maximum union is 7 (from x=2 and x=3 gives 7; from x=2 and x=1? but x=1 is already used). So we need to cover 10 points with 2 non-sunny and 2 sunny lines; but the 2 non-sunny lines can cover at most 7 points, leaving 3 points to be covered by 2 sunny lines. Those 3 points would be the ones not on the non-sunny lines. Could those 3 points be covered by 2 sunny lines? Possibly if they are collinear on one sunny line, then one sunny line covers them, and the other sunny line is redundant. So we need to see if there exists a selection of 2 non-sunny lines (from the families) such that the complement (points not on these lines) is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines (i.e., the complement is a union of two sunny lines). The complement size would be 10 - union. To have a chance, union should be at least 7, leaving at most 3 points. So we need union=7 or 8 or 9. We saw union=7 is possible (x=2 and x=3 gives 7). Then complement is 3 points: which points are they? The points with x≥2 are: (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). After covering x=2 and x=3, covered: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4) from x=2; (3,1),(3,2),(3,3) from x=3. So covered =7 points. The uncovered points are: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 3 points. Can these three points be covered by 2 sunny lines? They are (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Check slopes: (4,1)-(4,2) vertical; (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal; (4,2)-(5,1) slope -1. So no sunny line contains two of them. Thus each sunny line can cover at most 1 point, so we need 3 sunny lines. So fails.

What about other pairs of non-sunny lines? For instance, take x=2 and x+y=5? x=2 covers 4 points; x+y=5 covers (2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Overlap (2,3) is common, so union=4+3-1=6. Complement 4 points. Try x=2 and x+y=6? x=2 covers 4; x+y=6 covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Overlap (2,4) common, union=4+4-1=7. Covered: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Uncovered: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 3 points. Check slopes: (3,1)-(3,2) vertical; (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1. So no sunny line covers two. So need 3 sunny lines.

Try x=2 and y=2? Already did, union=7, uncovered 3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(3,3)? Actually y=2 gives (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2). Thus for n=5, no selection of 2 non-sunny lines gives a complement of size ≤4 that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. So k=2 fails.

In general, we can try to show that with n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover from the triangular grid (with x≥2) is something like (n-2)(n+1)/2 - something? Actually we can use the three families: the most points we can cover with n-2 lines is by taking the n-2 lines that are most "dense", which are the diagonals x+y = constant. For n, the points are sum ≤ n+1. The largest diagonals are sums n+1, n, ..., 2. Their sizes are n, n-1, ..., 1. So the sum of the sizes of the n-2 largest diagonals is sum_{i=2}^{n+1} (n+1-i) = sum_{j=1}^{n-1} j? Wait: For sum = n+1, size = n; sum = n, size = n-1; ... sum = 2, size = 1. The n-2 largest are from sum = n+1 down to sum = 3? Actually there are n diagonals (sum=2..n+1). The n-2 largest are sums n+1, n, ..., 4? Let's list: sums n+1, n, ..., 3? That's (n+1)-2 = n-1? Actually number of diagonals: sum=2 gives 1, sum=3 gives 2, ..., sum=n+1 gives n. So the n-2 largest are sums n+1, n, ..., 4? That's (n+1)-3 = n-2? Yes. Their sizes: n, n-1, ..., 3? Actually sum=4 gives size 3? Wait sum=4 gives points (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) =>3. So the sizes are n, n-1, ..., 3. Sum = sum_{j=3}^{n} j = (n(n+1)/2 - 1 - 2) = n(n+1)/2 - 3. That's the maximum number of points we can cover with n-2 lines of type slope -1 (since they are disjoint). For n=5, that's 15-3=12? Actually n(n+1)/2=15, minus 3 =12. But we only have 10 points with x≥2, so that's not applicable because we are covering the whole grid, not just x≥2. For the whole grid, the maximum union with n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3 (since N = n(n+1)/2, and sum of sizes of largest n-2 diagonals is n(n+1)/2 - 3). So that covers N-3 points, leaving 3 points. Those 3 points are the ones on the smallest diagonals: sum=2 and sum=3? Actually after using sums n+1 down to 4, the uncovered sums are sum=3 (2 points) and sum=2 (1 point) => total 3 points. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, we can always cover N-3 points, leaving exactly 3 points: (1,2),(2,1),(1,1). But we are not forced to use all these lines; we can choose a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines that might cover fewer points, leaving more points to be covered by sunny lines. However, we want to cover all points with n-2 non-sunny and 2 sunny lines. That means the set of points not on the non-sunny lines (the complement) must be a subset of the points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's denote the set of points covered by non-sunny lines as U. Then the complement C = P \ U must be coverable by 2 sunny lines. The size of C is N - |U|. Since we have 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points (if each has maximum). For n≥3, floor((n+1)/2) ≥ 2. So we need |C| ≤ 2 * floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 2*3=6. So C can have size up to 6. But we also have that |U| ≤ N-3 (since max union is N-3). So |C| ≥ 3. So the possible sizes are 3,4,5,6,... up to N? Actually |U| can be as low as something, but we are free to choose the non-sunny lines; they could be very "sparse" covering few points, leaving many points to be covered by sunny lines. However, we have only 2 sunny lines; they can cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points. For n=5, that's 6 points. So |C| ≤ 6. Therefore we need |U| ≥ N - 6 = 15-6=9. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least 9 points. For n=5, is it possible to cover 9 points with 3 non-sunny lines? Yes, we had examples: x=1, x=2, and x=3 cover 5+4+3=12 points? Actually x=1:5; x=2:4; x=3:3; total 12. So that's >9. So we can have |U| = 12, leaving 3 points; or |U| = 11, leaving 4; or |U| = 10, leaving 5; or |U| = 9, leaving 6. So we need to find a set of 3 non-sunny lines such that |U| is between 9 and 12, and the complement C is a subset of a union of two sunny lines. Since sunny lines are limited, we need to see if any such U exists.

For n=5, we attempted several sets of 3 non-sunny lines (including x=1, and other combinations). We found that the complement size was 3,4,5,6... but the complement sets were not unions of two sunny lines. We need to prove that for any n≥3, there is no covering with exactly 2 sunny lines.

We can try a more global argument: Consider the points with a=1. There are n points. Each sunny line can contain at most 1 of them. So the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from this column. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point from this column, because if it covers two points with a=1, then it would be vertical x=1 (which covers all n points) but then it would cover all of them, so we wouldn't need other lines for this column. Actually, a vertical line x=1 covers all n points. So if we use x=1 as a non-sunny line, we cover all n points. Then the sunny lines can cover points from other columns. So that's a good strategy. So assume we have x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. The points with a=1 are already covered.

Now, consider the points with a=2. There are n-1 points. They must be covered. Could we use x=2 as a non-sunny line? That would cover all of them. Then we would have n-4 non-sunny lines left. If we continue using all vertical lines up to x=n-2, we would use n-2 non-sunny lines (including x=1,...,n-2). But we only have n-2 non-sunny lines total. So we could use x=1,...,n-2 as non-sunny lines. That gives n-2 non-sunny lines covering all points with x≤n-2. Then the remaining points are those with x=n-1 and x=n. That's 3 points. They must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. As argued, 2 sunny lines cannot cover 3 points that are not collinear in a sunny way. So that fails. Therefore, we cannot use all vertical lines up to x=n-2. So at least one of the vertical lines x=i (for i=2,...,n-2) must be covered by sunny lines. But the sunny lines are only 2, and they can cover at most 2 points from the column x=i? Actually a sunny line can contain at most 1 point from a given vertical line, because if it contains two points with same x, it would be vertical (non-sunny). So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from each column. So with 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from each column. Therefore, if we need to cover points in a column that are not covered by non-sunny lines, we can use sunny lines to cover some of them. For example, in column x=2, there are n-1 points. If we don't use x=2 as a non-sunny line, we must cover these points using sunny lines and possibly other non-sunny lines. But other non-sunny lines (horizontal or diagonal) can cover at most 1 point from that column each. So to cover many points in a column, we need many lines.

Given that we have only 2 sunny lines, the total number of points we can cover from columns that are not fully covered by non-sunny lines is limited. This suggests that the non-sunny lines must cover most points in each column, and the sunny lines can only cover a few.

Maybe we can use a counting argument based on the "degree" of each point. Consider the number of points that are on the "boundary" of the triangular grid: the points with a=1, b=1, or a+b=n+1. There are n points with a=1, n points with b=1, and n points with a+b=n+1, but they overlap at corners. The total number of boundary points is something like 3n-3. For n=5, that's 12 points. These points must be covered. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from the set a=1? Actually each sunny line can contain at most 1 point from a=1 because if it contains two points with a=1, it would be vertical x=1, non-sunny. So the 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from the column a=1. Similarly, at most 2 from the row b=1. Also, from the diagonal a+b=n+1, each sunny line can contain at most 1 point because if it contains two points with same sum, it would be diagonal x+y=constant, non-sunny. So the 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from that diagonal. So the total number of boundary points that can be covered by sunny lines is at most? Actually they are overlapping, so we need to consider the union of the three boundary sets. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from each of the three families, but since the families intersect, the total number of boundary points that can be covered by the two sunny lines is at most? For example, a sunny line could go through (1,1) which is on all three families. So we need to count the maximum number of distinct boundary points that two sunny lines can cover. Since each sunny line is a line, it can intersect the boundary in at most some points. For n=5, the points on the boundary are: (1,1)-(1,5); (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 12 points. Can two sunny lines cover all 12? Probably not. But we need to see if the non-sunny lines can cover the rest.

This is getting too involved. Given the pattern and the difficulty, it's plausible that the answer is that k can be 0,1,3. But we need to be sure that k=3 is always possible. We have a construction for all n≥3. And we have examples for n=3,4,5,6 that k=2 is impossible. So the solution likely: k ∈ {0,1,3} for all n≥3.

However, let's test n=7 quickly with a systematic attempt: We want to cover 28 points with 2 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines. The maximum union with 5 non-sunny lines is 25 (by taking diagonals x+y=8,7,6,5,4). Then leftover 3 points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Can't cover with 2 sunny lines. So we need to use a different set of 5 non-sunny lines that covers 24 points, leaving 4 points. Can we achieve union=24 with 5 non-sunny lines? The largest total size for 5 disjoint lines is 25, so to get 24 we need one overlap. For example, take diagonals: x+y=8,7,6,5,3. Sizes: 7,6,5,4,2 sum=24. So union=24. Leftover points: those with sum=4 (3 points) and sum=2 (1 point) -> (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,1). Can these be covered by 2 sunny lines? As before, only sunny pair (1,1)-(2,2) slope1, leaving (1,3) and (3,1) uncovered. So no. What about using a mix of vertical and diagonal lines? For instance, take x=1 (5 points), x=2 (5? They are disjoint because x differ. So x=1 and x=2 cover 7+6=13 points. Then we have 3 non-sunny lines left. If we use x=3 (5 points), x=4 (4 points), x=5 (3 points) would be 5+4+3=12 points, total 25, leaving 3 points. Not good. Could we use a diagonal to reduce union? For instance, use x=1, x=2, and then a diagonal that overlaps with them to reduce union. For example, x=1 (7), x=2 (6), and x+y=5 (4 points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). Overlaps: x=1 shares (1,4) with x+y=5; x=2 shares (2,3); so union =7+6+4-2=15. That's too low. So we need union=24. That seems only possible by taking 5 disjoint lines of one type, with total size 24 (by reducing one diagonal by 1). But then leftover set is as above, which is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could we have leftover of size 5 or 6? For that, we would need union=23 or 22. That would require more overlaps. For example, take diagonals x+y=8,7,6,5,4 (sizes 7,6,5,4,3 sum=25). Actually after using sums 8,7,6,4,3, the uncovered sums are sum=5 (4 points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)), sum=2 (1 point: (1,1)), sum=1 none. So leftover 5 points. For instance, line y=x gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) but (2,2) not in set, (3,3) not, (4,4) not. So not. y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) -> (2,3) is in set; y=x-1 gives (2,1),(3,2) -> (3,2) in set; y=2x gives (1,2),(2,4) -> (2,4) not; y=2x+1 gives (1,3) -> not; y=-2x+7 gives (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) -> (2,3) in set; y=-2x+6 gives (1,4),(2,2),(3,0) -> (1,4) in set; y=-2x+5 gives (1,3),(2,1) -> not; y=-3x+8 gives (1,5),(2,2) -> not; y=-3x+7 gives (1,4),(2,1) -> (1,4) in set; y=-4x+9 gives (1,5) -> not; So we have several lines covering some points.
